Preferring Validity in Ambiguous Sarf Contracts
Refines the rule on ambiguous sarf contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for interpreting ambiguous sarf contracts and distinguish between permissible tashih (validating interpretation) and impermissible taghyir (changing the contract nature).

Core Idea
An ambiguous sarf contract must be interpreted in favor of validity, but cannot be altered in legal nature.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a hashiya on 'Al-Wiqayah,' refines a rule from 'Al-Kifayah' about sarf contracts. When the counter-exchange is absolute (unspecified), the jurist must attempt to interpret it as a valid exchange, but cannot arbitrarily change the contract's category. Understanding this balance is critical for correct fatwa in currency exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguity
Determine what is left unspecified in the contract, e.g., the type of currency or weight.
In sarf, ambiguity often arises when the counter-value is described as 'like this' or with a demonstrative.
2
Apply the principle of preferring validity
Interpret the absolute expression in the most natural way that yields a valid sarf contract, based on context and custom.
The default is to uphold the contract if a valid meaning is possible within the wording.
3
Recognize the limit: no taghyir
You cannot change the contract's legal character (e.g., turning it into a loan or a different sale) just to make it valid.
Taghyir al-taṣarruf is invalid; tashih must stay within the apparent meaning.
4
Conclude the ruling
If a valid interpretation is possible, the contract is sahih; otherwise it is fasid.
The interpreter must not assume invalidity hastily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : المقابلة المطلقة تحتمل الصرف المذكور وفي حذف الجنس إلى خلافِهِ تغيير تصرفه، وإثبات مقابلة لا دليل عليها في لفظه ، نعم فيه تصحيح تصرفه، ولكن تغيير التصرُّف لا يصح لتصحيح التصرف، كذا في «الكفاية».
We said: The absolute counter-exchange potentially implies the mentioned exchange. And in removing the type to something else, there is a change in its disposition, and establishing a counter-exchange for which there is no evidence in his words. Yes, in it there is correction of his disposition, but changing the disposition is not valid for correcting the disposition. Thus in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage states a key principle in Hanafi sarf (currency exchange): when a contract is ambiguous about the type of counter-value, one must interpret it in a way that makes the contract valid (sahih) rather than invalid (fasid). The author rejects the idea that one can arbitrarily change the nature of the contract to achieve validity; rather, the interpretation must be based on the apparent meaning. This is a ruling statement from the hashiya literature, refining the commentary on Al-Wiqayah.

Key Terms
المقابلة المطلقة (al-muqābalah al-muṭlaqah): The absolute counter-exchange, i.e., an exchange of one thing for another without specifying the type or proportion.
الصرف (al-ṣarf): The exchange of gold, silver, or currency; specifically, the sale of one type of currency for another.
تغيير التصرف (taghyīr al-taṣarruf): Changing the legal nature or effect of a transaction from its apparent meaning to something else.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not valid to change the legal nature of an ambiguous sarf contract?
A) Because the parties did not intend it.
B) Because it would alter the contract from its apparent meaning without evidence.
C) Because the contract is already valid.
D) Because the law requires a written agreement.

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous sarf contract must be interpreted in favor of validity, but the interpretation must not change the legal nature of the contract.

Preferring Valid Interpretation in Ambiguous Sarf Contracts
Providing logical and evidential reasons for interpreting ambiguous sarf contracts validly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of interpreting ambiguous sarf contract wording in favor of validity and apply it to the example of exchanging like currencies.

Core Idea
Always interpret ambiguous contract language to preserve validity, as long as the contract's essence remains unchanged.

When a sarf contract's wording is vague about the type of counter-value, Hanafi jurists insist on an interpretation that makes the contract valid. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya provides the rational proof: the contract inherently aims for a valid exchange, and only the attribute (describing counterpart) can be adjusted without altering the core transfer of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Recognize that the ambiguous wording can be interpreted as either an exchange of the whole for the whole (correcting) or as a pro-rata exchange of each part for each part (corrupting).
The corrupting interpretation leads to partial invalidity if not all parts are received.
2
Understand the limitation of the absolute
The contract implies absolute exchange without restrictions. However, in reality, every exchange must have a specific attribute. The absolute cannot be applied literally because existence without attribute is impossible.
This is a key usul principle: al-mutlaq cannot be implemented without taqyid.
3
Apply the principle of preserving validity
Since the absolute is inoperative, the contract is interpreted using a restricted (muqayyad) meaning. Choose the restriction that makes the contract valid (al-muqayyad al-musahhih) over the one that corrupts it, because the contract's aim is validity.
This is analogous to using a metaphorical meaning when the literal is impossible.
4
Analyze the example
Consider the case of selling two dinars for two dinars. Under the preferred interpretation, if each party takes one dinar and separates, the sale is valid in the entire taken portion (both dinars). Under the alternative interpretation, only half of each taken dinar would be valid.
The differing practical outcomes (thamarah) demonstrate the superiority of the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قلنا...الخ ؛ توضيحهُ : إِنَّ في صرف الجنس إلى خلافه تصحيح العقد، وإلى جنسه فساده، ولا معارضة بين الفاسد والصحيح ؛ لأنَّ الصحيح مشروع بأصلِهِ ووصفه ؛ والفاسد بأصله دون وصفه، ولأنَّ العقد يقتضي مطلق المقابلة من غير أن يتعرض لمقيد، لا مقابلة الكلِّ بالكلِّ بطريق الشيوع، ولا مقابلة الفرد من جنسه، ولا من خلافِ جنسيه ؛ لما عُرِفَ أنَّ المطلق غيرُ متعرض للمقيَّد، ولكن مع هذا عند الوجودِ لا يوجد إلا مقيداً ؛ لتعدرِ وجودِ ذات بدون صفة ، وإن كان اللفظ غير متعرض للصفةِ بل للذات فقط على ما عرف في موضعه. فيحمل على المقيد المصحح عند تعذر العمل بالإطلاق، ألا ترى أنه لو قال عند المقابلة : على أن يكون الجنس بخلاف الجنس صح، ولو كان منافياً لم يصح، فكان حمله على المقيَّد المصحح أولى من حمله على المقيَّدِ المفسد، وهو مقابلة الكلِّ بالكلِّ شائعاً، طلباً للصحة. ألا ترى أنَّ الكلام أصله أن يكون مستعملاً في حقيقته، ثم إذا تعذرت الحقيقة حمل على المجاز الممكن إذا كان لا يصح إلا بالحمل عليه، وإن كان تغيراً فهو تغيير للوصف لا تغير لأصل المقابلة، إذ هي موجودة؛ لأنَّ أصل المقابلة فيه إفادة الملكِ في الكلِّ بمقابلة الكل، وذلك لم يتغير. والدليل على أنه يحمل عليه عند التعدرِ طلباً للصحة أنه لو باع الجنس بالجنس بأن باع دينارين بدينارين مثلاً، فقبض كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ديناراً، ثمَّ افترقا صح البيع في المقبوض كله. ولو كان كما قالاه لما صح إلا في نصف كلِّ واحدٍ من المقبوضين؛ لأنَّ مقابلة
His statement: 'We said...' etc.; its clarification: Indeed, in changing the type to something else there is correction of the contract, and to its own type there is corruption, and there is no conflict between the corrupt and the correct because the correct is valid in its essence and attribute, while the corrupt is valid in its essence only without its attribute. And because the contract implies absolute counter-exchange without being restricted to any particular: not exchange of all for all by way of common ownership, nor exchange of an individual from its type, nor from a different type; because it is known that the absolute is not concerned with the restricted, but despite this when it comes into existence it only exists as restricted, because it is impossible for an essence to exist without an attribute, even if the wording is not concerned with the attribute but only the essence as known in its place. So it is interpreted according to the restricted interpretation that corrects it when acting upon the absolute is impossible. Do you not see that if he said at the time of the exchange: 'let the type be different from the type' it is valid, and if it were contradictory it would not be valid; thus interpreting it according to the correcting restriction is better than interpreting it according to the corrupting restriction, which is the exchange of all for all by common ownership, seeking validity. Do you not see that speech originally is to be used in its real meaning, then if the real meaning is impossible it is interpreted by a possible metaphorical meaning if it cannot be valid except by that interpretation, and even if it is a change, it is a change of the attribute not a change of the essence of the exchange, because the essence of the exchange is present; because the essence of the exchange is to effect ownership of all in exchange for all, and that has not changed. The evidence that it is interpreted in this way when impossible seeking validity is that if he sold one type for another type, such as selling two dinars for two dinars, and each of them took one dinar then separated, the sale is valid in all that was taken. If it were as they said, it would only be valid in half of each of the two items taken, because the exchange...
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed reasoning for the principle stated earlier. The commentator explains that when a contract's wording is ambiguous about the type of counter-value, one must interpret it in a way that makes the contract valid. The reasoning is based on the nature of contracts: a valid contract is sound in both essence and attribute, while a corrupt contract is sound only in essence. The contract implies an absolute exchange, but when it materializes, it must be restricted due to the impossibility of existence without attributes. Therefore, when the literal application is impossible, the contract is interpreted metaphorically to achieve validity, as long as the interpretation does not change the fundamental essence of the exchange (which is transfer of ownership). The proof given is an example: if someone sells two dinars for two dinars, and each party receives one dinar and then they part, the entire sale is valid in the received portion. If the opposite interpretation were used, only half would be valid because the exchange would be considered only in half. This shows that the preferred interpretation is the one that validates the contract.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-muṭlaq): An unqualified, unrestricted term that does not specify a particular attribute or restriction.
المقيد المصحح (al-muqayyad al-muṣaḥḥiḥ): The restricted interpretation that renders the contract valid (sahih).
الشيوع (al-shuyūʿ): Common ownership or shared proportion; here it refers to the interpretation that the exchange is of each part for a corresponding part (pro rata).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given in the passage for preferring the valid interpretation over the corrupt one?
A) It is more linguistically precise.
B) It preserves the essence of the contract while adjusting the attribute.
C) It avoids riba.
D) It is based on a hadith.

Key Takeaway
When a contract's wording is ambiguous, Hanafi law requires interpreting it to achieve validity, as long as the core exchange of ownership remains unchanged.

Validating Mixed Sarf Transactions: Matching Types and Avoiding Shuyu'
Presenting and validating specific sarf transactions involving mixed currency types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sarf transaction when different currency types are mixed, and explain how the Hanafi interpretation avoids partial invalidity.

Core Idea
In sarf, same types must be equal; different types can be freely exchanged—mixing is valid if each type is matched correctly.

This lesson examines three sarf transactions where dirhams, dinars, and produce are mixed. The Hanafi school upholds the validity of such mixed transactions by applying a specific interpretation: each currency type is matched with its own kind for equality, while different types are exchanged without the requirement of equality. This approach preserves the exchange's essence and prevents the invalidity that would result from a common-proportion (shuyu') interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Transactions
Note each case: (1) 11 dirhams for 10 dirhams + 1 dinar; (2) 1 good dirham + 2 defective dirhams for 2 good dirhams + 1 dirham; (3) produce for 2 good dirhams + 1 dirham.
Each transaction mixes different types or qualities.
2
Recognize the Core Principle
In sarf, same types (e.g., dirham for dirham) must be exchanged in equal amounts; different types (dirham for dinar, or currency for produce) have no equality requirement.
This is the key to validating the transactions.
3
Apply the Principle to Each Case
For case 1: 10 dirhams are matched with 10 dirhams (equal), leaving 1 dirham to be exchanged for the dinar (different type, no equality needed). For case 2: separate the good and defective dirhams accordingly. For case 3: the dirhams are matched with each other, and the produce is exchanged for the remaining dirham.
The matching must be explicit to avoid invalidity.
4
Understand the Avoidance of Shuyu'
The common-proportion interpretation would cause partial invalidity because it divides each item proportionally, potentially leaving some countervalue unmatched. The Hanafi method specifies a clear allocation, avoiding this.
Shuyu' is avoided because it corrupts the contract.
5
Conclude on the Exchange's Nature
The chosen interpretation does not change the essence of the exchange (sarf); it only specifies one of several possible meanings. This is permissible and necessary for validity.
Changing the essence would invalidate, but specification does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيع أحد عشر درهماً بعشرة دراهم ودينار وبيع درهم صحيح ودرهمين غلتين وبيع غلة بدرهمين صحيحين ودرهم وليس فيه تغيير صرفه ؛ لأنَّ موجبَهُ ثبوت الملكِ في الكلِّ بمقابلة الكل، فيكونُ الدرهمان في مقابلة الدينارين، والدينار في مقابلة الدرهم ، ويكون كُرُّ البر في مقابلة كُري الشعير، وكُرُ الشَّعير في مقابلة كُري البر. وبيع أحد عشر درهماً بعشرة دراهم ودينار) بأن يكون عشرة دراهم بعشرة دراهم بقي درهم بمقابلة دينار. وبيع درهم صحيح و درهمين غلتين بدرهمين صحيحين ودرهم الشيوع يقتضي أن يكون نصفه مقابلاً بالمقبوض ، ونصف بغير المقبوض ، فيبطل حصة غير المقبوض . قوله : وليس فيه تغيير صرفه ؛ يعني ليس فيه تغيير كلامه، بل هو تعيين أحد المحتملين، ولئن كان فيه تغییر، ففيه تغيير وصفه.
The sale of eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar, and the sale of one authentic dirham and two defective dirhams for two authentic dirhams and one dirham, and the sale of produce for two authentic dirhams and a dirham, and there is no change in its exchange because its requirement is the establishment of ownership of all in exchange for all; thus two dirhams are in exchange for two dinars, and the dinar is in exchange for the dirham, and a kurr of wheat is in exchange for two kurrs of barley, and a kurr of barley is in exchange for two kurrs of wheat. And the sale of eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar: it is that ten dirhams are for ten dirhams, leaving one dirham in exchange for the dinar. And the sale of one authentic dirham and two defective dirhams for two authentic dirhams and a dirham: common proportion (shuyu') would require that half of it be in exchange for what is taken, and half for what is not taken, so the portion of what is not taken becomes void. His statement: 'And there is no change in its exchange' means there is no change in his speech, rather it is specification of one of the probabilities; and even if there is a change, it is a change of its attribute.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents three specific sarf transactions and validates them under Hanafi principles. The first is selling eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar. The second is selling one good dirham and two defective dirhams for two good dirhams and one dirham. The third is selling produce for two good dirhams and a dirham. The key principle is that when different types are mixed, the transaction is interpreted so that same types are exchanged equally and different types are exchanged freely. For example, in the first transaction, ten dirhams are matched with ten dirhams (equal and same type), leaving one dirham to be exchanged for the dinar (different type, no equality required). The commentary emphasizes that this does not change the essence of the exchange (sarf) but only specifies the intended meaning. If the interpretation were based on common proportion (shuyu'), the transaction would be partially invalid because some part would be uncaptured. The preferred interpretation validates the entire contract.

Key Terms
الغلة (al-ghullah): Produce or yield from land; a type of commodity that can be exchanged in sarf-like transactions.
الشيوع (al-shuyūʿ): The interpretation that each part of an asset corresponds proportionally to the counter-value; here it leads to partial invalidity.
تغيير الصرف (taghyīr al-ṣarf): Changing the nature of the exchange (sarf) itself; the passage argues that the chosen interpretation does not change the essence of the exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sale of eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar, how does the Hanafi interpretation validate the transaction?
A) All eleven dirhams are exchanged for the dinar and ten dirhams proportionally
B) Ten dirhams are matched with ten dirhams (equal), and the remaining dirham is exchanged for the dinar (different type, no equality required)
C) The dinar is ignored and only the dirhams are considered
D) The transaction is declared invalid due to inequality

Key Takeaway
Mixed sarf transactions are valid when same types are matched equally and different types are exchanged freely, and this interpretation does not change the nature of the exchange.

Rulings on Sarf: Equality, Defective Coins, and Set-off by Istihsan
Three currency exchange cases with proofs and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings on sarf (mixed currencies, defective coins, and set-off) and apply the hadith 'silver for silver, like for like' to identify when equality is required.

Core Idea
In sarf, equality is required only for same currency; set-off by istihsan overrides strict qiyas.

Currency exchange (sarf) has strict rules to prevent riba. This lesson unpacks three practical cases from a Hanafi commentary: selling mixed quantities of dirhams and dinars, transacting defective (cut) coins by weight, and a set-off scenario where a debtor buys a dinar from his creditor. Each case tests the principle of equality (tamathul) and the role of juristic preference (istihsan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case: mixing currencies
Read the first ruling: selling 11 dirhams for 10 dirhams and a dinar. Determine how the equality condition applies.
The 10 dirhams match 10 dirhams; the extra dirham is for the dinar (different type).
2
Analyse the second case: defective coins
Examine the sale of sound and defective dirhams. Understand why part of the transaction becomes void due to joint ownership.
Joint ownership (shuyu') leads to partial voidness when the received coin partly pays for an unreceived part.
3
Evaluate the third case: set-off
Work through the scenario: a debtor owes 10 dirhams and buys a dinar for 10 dirhams unconditionally. Note the validity by istihsan versus Zufar's objection by qiyas.
Istihsan validates because the set-off is interpreted as a rescission and new exchange.
4
Map the dalil
Identify the hadith 'Silver for silver, like for like' as the proof for equality in same-type currencies.
The hadith is used to require tamathul for dirham-dirham exchanges.
5
Contrast Hanafi and Zufar's positions
Note that Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) adopt istihsan for the set-off case, while Zufar applies strict qiyas.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.

(۳) قوله : وبيع ... الخ ؛ أي صح هذا البيع، قال صاحب الايضاح»: ذكر صاحب «الوقاية» هاهنا مسائل من مسائل الربا، ورددناها إلى بابها. انتهى. ويمكن الجواب بأنَّ التماثل قد شرط في الصرف احترازاً عن الفضل الجودي إلى الربا، فوجه ذكر هذه المسائل الثلاثة في الصرف أنَّ مبناه على الجواز، لا في باب الربا، فإنَّ مبناه على عدم
الجواز فافهم ولا تعجل.

كتاب الصرف تعريفه وأحكامه
۳۰۹
وبيع من عليه عشرة دراهم ممن هي له ديناراً بها مطلقة إن دفع الدينار وتقاصا
العشرة بالعشرة الغلة : ما يردُّه بيت المال، ويأخذه التجار ، وإنّما يجوز هذا لتحققِ التّساوي في الوزن، وسقوط اعتبار الجودة.
( وبيع من عليه عشرة دراهم ممن هي له ديناراً بها مطلقة إن دفع الدينار وتقاصا العشرة بالعشرة ) : أي لزيدٍ على عمرو عشرة دراهم، فباغ عمرو دينارا
من زيد بعشرة مطلقة: أي لم يضف العقدَ بالعشرةِ التي على عمرو ، صح البيع إن دَفَعَ عمرو الدينار ، فصار بكلِّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخر عشرة دراهم فتقاصاً العشرة بالعشرة، فيكون هذا التَّقاص فسخاً للبيع الأول، وهو بيع الدينار بالعشرة المطلقة، وبيعا للدينار بالعشرة التي على عمرو إذ لو لم يحمل على هذا لكان استدالاً بدل الصرف [١] قوله : ما يرده بيت المال ؛ لا للزيافة، بل لأنها درهم مقطعة مكسرة، تكون القطعة منها ربعاً وثمناً وأقل، وبيت المال لا يأخذ إلا العالي، ذكره شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشية الدر المختار)) ، والله أعلم بحقائق الأسرار.
[٢] قوله : مطلقة ؛ أي من غير أن تقيَّد بالعشرة التي عليه، أما إذا قيَّد بذلك فقال : بالعشرة التي عليه، يجوزُ البيع بلا خلاف، وبخلاف المطلقة فإنّه جائز عندنا ، خلافاً لزفر . كذا في «النهاية».
[۳] قوله : صح البيع ؛ هذا الاستحسان ، والقياس أن لا يجوز، وهو قولُ هو زفره ؛ لأنه يكون استبدالاً ببدل الصرف ؛ لأنَّ الذي وجب بالصرف غير الذي كان عليه ؛ وهذا لأنه وجب بالصرف دين يجب تعيينه بالقبض احترازاً عن الربا، والدين الذي كان عليه لا يجب قبضه ، فكانا غَيْرَيْن.
ألا ترى أنَّ المقاصة لا تقعُ بنفس العقدِ لعدم المجانسة، فيكون التقاص بعد ذلك استبدالاً يبدل الصرف ؛ لأنه أخذ ما في ذمَّتِهِ بدل ما وجب له وعليه من ثمن الدينار، فلا
يجوز، ولهذا لا يجوز في رأس مال السلم.
(۱) (حاشية الطحطاوي على الدر» (۳: ١٤٠).
Essence of al-Nihayah for Umdat al-Ri'ayah: Regarding eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar, and the sale of a sound dirham and two defective dirhams for two sound dirhams and a dirham, and the sale of a defective dirham for two sound dirhams and a dirham, and there is no change of its exchange; because its requirement is the establishment of ownership in all in exchange for all, so the two dirhams are in exchange for the two dinars, and the dinar is in exchange for the dirham, and it would be like a kurr of wheat in exchange for two kurr of barley, and a kurr of barley in exchange for two kurr of wheat. And the sale of eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar: such that ten dirhams are for ten dirhams, leaving a dirham in exchange for a dinar. And the sale of a sound dirham and two defective dirhams for two sound dirhams and a dirham: joint ownership requires that half be in exchange for what is received and half for what is not received, so the share of what is not received becomes void. And if you want more detail, refer to 'al-Tabyin' and 'al-Bahr'. [1] His saying: 'and there is no change of its exchange' means there is no change of his speech, rather it is specifying one of the two possibilities. And if there is change, then it changes its description. [2] His saying: 'and the sale...etc.' means this sale is valid, and the ten dirhams are for their like, and the dinar for a dirham; because the condition in the sale of dirhams is equality, due to his saying: 'Silver for silver, like for like.' And it is apparent that the contracting party intended equality, because speech is interpreted as valid rather than invalid. So the dirham remains for the dinar, they being two different types, and equality is not considered in them. He only mentioned this issue after the preceding one, although it is known from before, to show that it is not required that the two types be from both sides, but if they are on one side, the ruling is similarly. (3) His saying: 'and the sale...etc.' means this sale is valid. The author of al-Idah said: The author of al-Wiqayah mentioned here issues of riba, and we have referred them back to their chapter. End quote. And it can be answered that equality is stipulated in sarf to avoid excess leading to riba, so the reason for mentioning these three issues in sarf is that its foundation is permissibility, not in the chapter of riba, for its foundation is impermissibility. So understand and do not hasten. Chapter of Sarf: Its Definition and Rulings. And the sale of a person who owes ten dirhams to someone who has a claim of a dinar against him unconditionally, if he pays the dinar and they set off ten dirhams for ten dirhams. The defective coin is what the treasury returns and merchants take. And it is only permitted due to the verification of equality in weight and disregarding quality. And the sale of a person who owes ten dirhams to someone who has a claim of a dinar against him unconditionally, if he pays the dinar and they set off ten dirhams for ten dirhams: meaning Zaid has ten dirhams owed by Amr. Then Amr bought a dinar from Zaid for ten dirhams unconditionally, i.e., he did not tie the contract to the ten dirhams that Amr owes. The sale is valid if Amr pays the dinar, so each of them now has ten dirhams against the other, so they set off ten dirhams for ten dirhams. This set-off is a rescission of the first sale, and it is a sale of the dinar for the unconditional ten dirhams, and a sale of the dinar for the ten dirhams that Amr owed. If it were not interpreted this way, it would be an exchange of the substitute of sarf. [1] His saying: 'what the treasury returns' is not because they are counterfeit, but because they are cut-up broken dirhams, a piece being a quarter, an eighth, or less, and the treasury only takes high-quality ones. Our teacher al-Allamah al-Tahawi mentioned this in his hashya on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And Allah knows best the realities of secrets. [2] His saying: 'unconditionally' means without tying to the ten dirhams that he owes. However, if he ties it to that by saying 'for the ten that he owes', the sale is permitted without disagreement. As for the unconditional case, it is permitted according to us, contrary to Zufar. Thus in al-Nihayah. [3] His saying: 'the sale is valid' is istihsan. The analogy is that it is not allowed, and that is the saying of Zufar, because it would be an exchange of the substitute of sarf; because that which is due by sarf is other than that which was owed. This is because by sarf there becomes due a debt that must be specified by receipt to avoid riba, while the debt that was owed does not require receipt, so they are two different things. Do you not see that set-off does not occur by the contract itself due to lack of homogeneity, so the set-off afterwards is an exchange of the substitute of sarf, because he takes what is in his liability in exchange for what was due to him and against him from the price of the dinar, so it is not allowed. For this reason it is not allowed in the capital of salam. (1) (Hashiyat al-Tahawi 'ala al-Durr, 3:140).
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from a Hanafi fiqh commentary on al-Wiqayah, discussing three key rulings in the chapter of sarf (currency exchange). The first ruling concerns selling eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar. The commentator explains that this is valid because the ten dirhams are exchanged for ten dirhams (meeting the condition of equality for same currency), and the remaining dirham is exchanged for the dinar (different currencies, so equality not required). The proof cited is the hadith 'Silver for silver, like for like.' The commentator clarifies that the contract is interpreted as intending equality to uphold validity. The second ruling involves selling one sound dirham and two defective dirhams for two sound dirhams and one defective dirham. Due to joint ownership (shuyu'), half of the defective dirham received is considered in exchange for the unreceived defective dirham, leading to voidness of that portion. The third ruling is a case of set-off: a debtor of ten dirhams buys a dinar from his creditor for ten dirhams unconditionally. If the debtor pays the dinar, the sale is valid and they set off the ten dirhams each owes, which effectively rescinds the first sale. This is istihsan; the qiyas (analogy) would invalidate it, as held by Zufar, because the debt from sarf differs from the pre-existing debt. The passage also defines 'ghallah' (defective coin) as what the treasury rejects. The commentary emphasizes that these issues are placed in sarf because its basis is permissibility, unlike riba.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Exchange of currency or gold/silver for the same or different types, with specific legal conditions.
تماثل (tamathul): Equality in weight for the same type of currency (e.g., silver dirham for silver dirham).
غلة (ghallah): A defective dirham that is broken or cut; the treasury rejects it; merchants still accept it at weight.
استحسان (istihsan): A juristic preference that overrides qiyas for a more suitable ruling; used here to validate the set-off sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on a person who owes ten dirhams buying a dinar from the creditor for ten dirhams (unconditionally), and then paying the dinar and setting off the debts?
A) The sale is invalid both by qiyas and istihsan.
B) The sale is valid by istihsan, and the set-off constitutes a rescission of the sale and a new exchange.
C) The sale is valid only if the ten dirhams are specifically tied to the debt.
D) The sale is invalid because equality in weight is not observed.

Key Takeaway
In sarf, equality is required for same type; istihsan may override qiyas to validate beneficial transactions.

Mixed Dirhams: Currency or Commodity?
Ruling on mixed dirham exchange and commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging mixed dirhams with pure silver and identify when a coin is treated as currency (thaman) vs. commodity (ʿaraḍ).

Core Idea
If adulteration dominates, a coin is a commodity—sarf rules no longer apply.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya addresses a practical issue: when people use coins that are not pure but contain an alloy (ghish). You need to know when such coins are still considered currency subject to the strict rules of ṣarf, and when they become mere goods. The Hanafi school uses the principle of predominant element to decide.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between two cases: (1) adulteration is slight and the silver is predominant, and (2) adulteration is predominant (al-ghālib). In the first, the coin remains a currency; in the second, it becomes a commodity.
The turning point is when the ghish overwhelms the pure metal, making the silver 'consumed' (mustahlak).
2
Determine the ruling for the first scenario
When the coin is still currency (ghish negligible), the rules of ṣarf apply: exchange must be hand-to-hand (qabḍ in session) and equal by weight if the same genus (silver for silver). Selling a mixed dirham for pure silver must meet these conditions.
The passage mentions the 'sword ornament' example: pure silver is compared to silver in the mixed dirham; if equal or less, invalid; if more, valid with qabḍ.
3
Determine the ruling for the second scenario
When adulteration dominates, the mixture is not considered a currency but a commodity (ʿaraḍ). The sale is governed by general contract rules, not ṣarf. Conditions like equal weight and hand-to-hand no longer apply.
The commentator al-Aqṭaʿ adds: this applies only if the silver cannot be separated without difficulty; if separable, it is not consumed and remains currency.
4
Apply the principle 'al-ghālib yulḥaqu bi-hi al-maghloob'
The ruling is based on the maxim that the predominant element controls. The overwhelmed (maghlūb) is treated as nonexistent. This is why the small amount of ghish is ignored, but dominant ghish changes the legal nature.
This maxim appears in other areas of fiqh, e.g., in determining the ruling of mixed foods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يجز بيع الخالصين به، ولا بيع بعضه ببعض إلا متساوياً وزناً، وإن غلب عليهما الغش فهما في حكم العرضين، فبيعُهُ بالفضة الخالصة على وجوه : حلية السيف) : أي إن كانت الفضة الخالصة مثل الفضة التي في الدراهم، أو أقل أو لا يدري لا يصح ، وإن كانت أكثر يصح إن لم يفترقا بلا قبض. عن الدرهمية، أو الدينار عن الدينارية ؛ لأنَّ النقود المستعملة بين الناس لا تخلو منه، لأنها لا تنطبع إلا مع الغش، فإنّها بدون بعض الغش لا يجتمع بعضها مع بعض. وقد يكون الغش خلقياً كما في ناقص العيار فيلحق القليلُ بالرداءة الفطرية، والجيد والرديء سواء عند المقابلة بالجنس، فيجعلُ الغش الذي فيهما معدوماً، حتى لا يكون له اعتبار أصلا بخلاف ما إذا كان الغالب هو الغش.
11) قوله : وإن غلب ...الخ ؛ قال العلامة الأَقْطَعُ في «شرح مختصر القُدُوري»: هذا إذا كانت الفضَّةُ لا تتخلّص عن الغش ؛ لأنها صارت مستهلكة، ولا اعتبار لها، أما إذا كانت تتخلّص عن الغش فليست مستهلكة. انتهى.
[٢]قوله : في [حكم العرضين ؛ اعتباراً للغالب، فإنَّ المغلوب في مقابلة الغالب كالمستهلك، قال شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي (١) : مقتضى ذلك أنه لا يشترط القبض، ولا يشترط أن يكون الخالص أكثر إلا أن يجاب بأنَّ المراد بذلك أنه ليس في حكم الصرف. انتهى. فالأولى تعبير «الكنز» (۲) بقوله : ليس في حكم الدراهم والدنانير. (۳) قوله : لا يصح؛ العقد في الفضة ولا في النحاس إذا كان لا تتخلص الفضة إلا بضرر، وإن كانت الفضَّةُ الخالصة أكثرَ ما في الدراهم يصح ليكون ما في الدراهم من الفضة بمثلها من الخالصة، والزائد [من الخالصة بمقابلة الغش، كذا في «الفتح». (1) في حاشيته على الدر المختار» (۳: ١٤٠). (۲) «کنز الدقائق»(ص۱۰۹). (۳) «فتح القدير» (٦ : ٢٧٥).
It is not permissible to sell two pure (silver coins) with it (i.e., mixed dirhams), nor is it permissible to sell some of them (mixed dirhams) with others except equally by weight. And if adulteration (ghish) dominates them, they are in the ruling of commodities (ʿurūḍ). So selling it (the mixed dirham) for pure silver has aspects: sword ornament (ḥilyat al-sayf): i.e., if the pure silver is like the silver in the dirhams, or less, or unknown, it is not valid; and if it is more, it is valid provided they do not separate without delivery (qabḍ). (This is) because the coins used among people are not free from adulteration, because they cannot be minted without adulteration, since without some adulteration they would not cohere. And the adulteration may be natural, as in deficient standard, so the small amount (of impurity) is included in natural defect, and the good and bad are equal when compared by genus, so the adulteration in them is considered non-existent, so that it has no consideration at all, contrary to when the adulteration is predominant. 11) His statement: 'And if adulteration dominates...' etc.; al-ʿAllāmah al-Aqṭaʿ said in Sharḥ Mukhtaṣar al-Qudūrī: This applies when the silver cannot be separated from the adulteration because it has become consumed (mustahlaka) and has no consideration; but if it can be separated, it is not consumed. End. [2] His statement: 'in the ruling of commodities' — considering the predominant, for the overwhelmed in comparison to the predominant is like consumed. Our teacher al-ʿAllāmah al-Ṭaḥtāwī said: The implication is that delivery (qabḍ) is not required, nor is it required that the pure (silver) be more, except that it may be answered that what is meant is that it is not in the ruling of ṣarf. End. So the better expression of al-Kanz is his statement: it is not in the ruling of dirhams and dinars. (3) His statement: 'it is not valid' — the contract is in the silver and not in the copper if the silver cannot be separated except with difficulty; but if the pure silver is more than what is in the dirhams, it is valid so that the silver in the dirhams is matched by the same amount of pure silver, and the extra pure silver is in exchange for the adulteration, as in al-Fatḥ.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary on the permissibility of exchanging dirhams that contain an admixture of base metal (ghish). The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: if the adulteration is insignificant (the silver is predominant), then the dirham is still treated as a currency (thaman) and subject to the strict rules of ṣarf (currency exchange): it must be exchanged hand-to-hand and equally by weight if of the same genus. If the adulteration dominates (ghalaba al-ghish), then the mixture is no longer considered currency but rather a commodity (ʿaraḍ), and the rules of ṣarf do not apply. The commentary then clarifies the case of selling a mixed dirham for pure silver, particularly when the pure silver is in the form of a sword ornament. The ruling is that if the pure silver is equal to or less than the silver content in the mixed dirham, the sale is invalid because it would involve ribā (usury). If it is more, it is valid, provided immediate delivery (qabḍ) occurs in the session, because the excess is considered in exchange for the copper content. The commentary by al-Aqṭaʿ and al-Ṭaḥtāwī emphasizes that the consideration is whether the silver can be recovered from the mixture; if it can be separated without difficulty, the mixture is not considered consumed (mustahlaka) and retains its currency status. This ruling is a practical application of the Hanafi principle that the predominant element determines the legal status of a mixture.

Key Terms
غش (ghish): Adulteration or alloy mixed into the metal of a coin, usually a base metal like copper added to silver or gold.
الخالصين (al-khāliṣayn): The two pure (metals): i.e., pure gold and pure silver coins without any adulteration.
العرضين (al-ʿaraḍayn): Commodities (dual form) — goods that are not currency, such as copper or brass items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a mixed dirham in which adulteration (ghish) is predominant is treated as what?
A) Currency (thaman) subject to ṣarf rules
B) A commodity (ʿaraḍ) not subject to ṣarf rules
C) Void (bāṭil) and cannot be sold
D) Pure silver (khāliṣ)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the legal status of a mixed dirham depends on the predominant element: if ghish dominates, it's a commodity; if silver dominates, it's currency subject to ṣarf rules.

Selling Mixed Dirhams of Same Kind with Excess
Ruling on selling mixed dirhams of the same genus with excess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling mixed dirhams of the same genus with excess and explain the condition of immediate delivery and the rationale based on the composite nature of the coins.

Core Idea
Mixed dirham sales allow excess because the mixture includes both silver and base metal; the excess is for the base metal.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, the exchange of currencies (ṣarf) is strictly regulated to prevent ribā. However, when dirhams are heavily adulterated with copper or brass, they are no longer considered pure silver. This lesson examines the ruling that such mixed dirhams can be sold with excess in weight or count, provided the parties take immediate possession (qabḍ) in the same session. The commentary explains the reasoning: the mixture is treated as two separate items—silver and base metal—so the excess is justified as payment for the base metal part. The lesson also covers a precautionary view from later Hanafi scholars who forbade excess in high-value circulating coins to prevent opening the door to ribā.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling heavily mixed dirhams of the same genus with an excess in weight or count. The ruling is that it is valid (ṣaḥiḥ) subject to the condition of immediate delivery (qabḍ) in the session.
The ruling applies when the adulteration predominates (ghalaba al-ghish).
2
Understand the condition
The condition is qabḍ fī al-majālis: both parties must take and give possession of the dirhams before they separate from the contract session. This condition applies because the exchange involves both currency (silver) and commodity (copper), and for currency exchange (ṣarf), immediate delivery is required.
If delivery is not immediate, the contract is invalid (bāṭil) because of ribā.
3
Analyze the rationale (taʿlīl)
The mixture is considered to consist of two distinct types: silver (thaman) and base metal (ṣufr). Since the genus differs, excess is not ribā. The excess is assigned to the base metal part, which is a commodity, not currency.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between pure currency and composite items.
4
Note the precautionary view
In al-Kāfī and al-Hidāyah, early Hanafi scholars (mashāyikh) prohibited excess in specific high-value coins (al-ʿadalī and al-ʿaṭārifa) because they were the most precious and widely circulated, so allowing excess could lead to ribā. This shows that the ruling may vary with custom and the nature of the coins.
This is a matter of iḥtiyāṭ (precaution) and is not the predominant position but is relied upon in certain contexts.
5
Distinguish the case where silver is destroyed by melting
If the silver in the mixture burns away completely upon melting, the mixture is treated as pure copper, and selling it with excess of the same genus becomes prohibited (ḥarām). This exception is based on the fact that the mixture no longer retains any silver value.
The ruling depends on whether the silver is recoverable; if not, the item is simply a base metal commodity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[2] (وإن شرى سلعة بالدراهم المغشوشة أو بالفلوس النافقة صح ، فإن كَسَدَت ١ ] قوله : وبجنسه...الخ ؛ أي إن بيعت الدراهم التي غلب غشها بجنسها متفاضلا ، راء كان عدداً أو وزناً صح ذلك البيع ؛ لأنَّ الغش من كلِّ واحدٍ منهما مقابل بالفضَّةِ أو الذهب الذي في الآخر فلا يضرُّ التفاضل فيهما باختلاف الجنس. سوا وفي «الكافي»: لم يجوز مشايخنا التفاضل في العدالى والعَطَارِفة ؛ لأنها أعزُّ الأموال وأروجها في ديارنا، فيفضي إباحته إلى فتح باب الربا. انتهى، وهكذا في «الهداية»(۱)، وغيرها.
[٢) قوله: صفر ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير» (۲) : الصفر مثل قفل ، وكسر الصادِ لغة : النحاس، وقيل: أجوده. انتهى. وقال في «الصحاح (۳) : بالضم الذي يعمل منه الأواني. انتهى. وبهندي : كاشي گويند. كذا في «الغياث».

And (selling it) with its own kind with excess is valid on condition of delivery (qabḍ) in the sessions. And indeed, exchange (ṣarf) from genus to a different genus is valid because it is in the ruling of two things: silver and brass (ṣufr). So if delivery is required in silver, it is required in brass due to lack of distinction. [2] (And if he buys a commodity with mixed dirhams or with legal tender fulūs, it is valid; but if they become demonetized (kasād) ... 1] His statement: 'And with its own kind...' i.e., if dirhams whose adulteration predominates are sold with their own kind with excess, whether by count or weight, that sale is valid because the adulteration from each is offset by the silver or gold in the other, so the excess between them does not harm due to difference of genus. Similarly, in al-Kāfī: Our scholars did not permit excess in al-ʿadalī (a type of dirham) and al-ʿaṭārifa because they are the most precious and most commonly circulated properties in our lands, so allowing it would lead to opening the door of ribā. End. And similarly in al-Hidāyah and others. [2] His statement: 'ṣufr'; he said in al-Miṣbāḥ al-Munīr: al-ṣufr (like a lock) and breaking the ṣād is a language: copper, and it is said: its best. End. And he said in al-Ṣiḥāḥ: with ḍamma, that from which utensils are made. End. And in Hindi: kāshī is said. So in al-Ghiyāth. [3] His statement: 'condition in ṣufr'; this applies when it is known that the silver collects when the mixed is melted and does not burn; but if it is known that it burns and is destroyed, its ruling is the ruling of pure copper, and it is not permissible to sell it with its own kind with excess, as in Majmaʿ al-Anhur and others.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of heavily adulterated dirhams of the same genus (e.g., silver dirhams with copper admixture being sold for each other) with an excess (tafāḍul). The ruling is that such a sale is valid provided immediate delivery (qabḍ) takes place in the session. The reason given is that the mixture is considered to consist of two components: silver (a currency) and copper (a commodity). Therefore, the excess is permissible because it is essentially an exchange of copper for silver, not silver for silver. The commentary then cites a caution from al-Kāfī and al-Hidāyah: the early scholars (mashāyikh) did not permit excess in certain high-quality coins (al-ʿadalī and al-ʿaṭārifa) because they were the most valuable and widely circulated; allowing excess could lead to ribā. This shows that the ruling may vary based on custom and the nature of the coins. The passage further clarifies that if the silver in the mixture cannot be recovered by melting (i.e., it burns away), the mixture is treated as pure copper, and selling it with its own kind with excess is prohibited. This is a nuanced application of the principles of ṣarf and the treatment of mixed substances in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
صفر (ṣufr): Copper or brass; in this context, it refers to the base metal component of a mixed coin.
القبض في المجالس (al-qabḍ fī al-majālis): Delivery (of the goods) in the same session (majlis) of the contract, a requirement for validity in ṣarf.
تفاضل (tafāḍul): Excess in quantity or weight when exchanging items of the same genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it permissible to sell heavily mixed dirhams of the same genus with excess?
A) Because all dirhams are considered pure silver
B) Because the admixture makes them a different genus
C) Because the excess is considered to be in exchange for the base metal component
D) Because immediate delivery removes the element of ribā

Key Takeaway
Selling mixed dirhams of the same genus with excess is valid if immediate delivery occurs, because the excess is attributed to the base metal component; but caution is advised for highly circulated coins to prevent ribā.

Purchase with Mixed Dirhams or Fulūs and the Effect of Kasād
Ruling on sale with mixed dirhams/fulūs and kasād

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a sale made with mixed dirhams or fulūs, and explain the seller's option (khiyār) when the currency becomes universally demonetized before payment.

Core Idea
A sale with current currency is valid; if the currency is demonetized before payment, the seller can cancel.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario in sales (buyū‘): purchasing goods using currency that later loses its legal tender status. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the initial validity of the contract and the subsequent option granted to the seller to protect him from loss. We will explore the strict definition of kasād and its legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and initial ruling
The act is purchasing goods with mixed dirhams (where silver content is low) or with fulūs that are currently legal tender. The initial ruling is that the sale is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) because the currency functions as thaman (price).
Mixed dirhams (maghshūshah) are still considered dirhams if they are accepted in transactions.
2
Understand the condition that triggers a problem
A problem arises if after the contract is concluded, but before the seller receives payment, the currency becomes demonetized (kasād). This means it is no longer accepted as a medium of exchange.
The seller's right to payment is in that specific currency; if it loses value, the contract may become defective.
3
Define kasād as given in the commentary
The commentary defines kasād strictly: the currency must be abandoned in all countries. If it still circulates in some countries, the sale remains valid but becomes defective locally, giving the seller an option.
'All countries' is a high threshold; mere local demonetization does not automatically void the contract.
4
Determine the legal effect
If kasād is universal and the seller has not yet received the price, the seller has an option (khiyār) to rescind or confirm the sale. This is analogous to the option for defect (khiyār al-ʿayb).
The option is only for the seller, not the buyer, because the seller would suffer loss from accepting worthless currency.
5
Apply the ruling to practical cases
If the currency is demonetized only locally, the seller still has the option because the currency is defective in their country. If demonetization is universal, the option is more clearly activated.
The option must be exercised; if the seller does nothing, the sale remains valid but the currency is worthless—so the seller should cancel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شرى سلعة بالدراهم المغشوشة أو بالفلوس النافقة صح، فإن كَسَدَت [١] قوله : وبجنسه...الخ [مكرر] لكن هذا الجزء مخصص للفقرة الثالثة. النص المطلوب: وإن شرى سلعة بالدراهم المغشوشة أو بالفلوس النافقة صح، فإن كَسَدَت (أي تروك المعاملة بها في جميع البلاد) تخير البائع (إن لم يقبض الثمن). والمذكور هنا في المتن: وإن شرى سلعة بالدراهم المغشوشة أو بالفلوس النافقة صح ، فإن كَسَدَت. ثم الشرح: [٤]قوله : فإن كسدت ؛ حد الكسادِ أن تترك المعاملة بها في جميع البلاد، وإن كان يروج في بعض البلاد، لا يبطل البيع، لكنّه يتعيَّبُ إذا لم تروج في بلدهم، فيتخيرُ البائع (إذا لم يقبض الثمن).
And if he buys a commodity with mixed dirhams or with legal tender fulūs, it is valid; but if they become demonetized (kasād) ... [4] His statement: 'if they become demonetized'; the definition of kasād is that dealing with them is abandoned in all countries. If they are still in circulation in some countries, the sale is not invalidated, but it becomes defective if they are not circulated in their country; then the seller has an option (if he has not received the price).
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage rules that purchasing goods using heavily adulterated dirhams (ghish predominant) or using fulūs (copper coins that are currently legal tender) is a valid transaction. However, a problem arises if after the contract but before payment (qabḍ), those coins become demonetized (kasād). The definition of kasād given by the commentators is that the coins are no longer accepted in transactions in all countries. If they still circulate in some countries, the sale remains valid. But if they are not accepted in the local country, the seller (who is expecting payment in those coins) has an option (khiyār) to cancel or confirm the sale, provided he has not yet received the price. This is because the value of the coins has effectively become worthless, and enforcing the contract would cause harm to the seller. This ruling applies the Hanafi principle of khiyār al-ʿayb (option of defect) by analogy, treating the demonetization as a defect in the currency.

Key Terms
الفلوس النافقة (al-fulūs al-nāfiqah): Copper coins that are legally current (nāfiq) as a medium of exchange in the market.
الكساد (al-kasād): Demonetization; the cessation of use of a currency in all transactions in all countries.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The seller's option to rescind or confirm the sale upon occurrence of kasād before receiving payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a buyer purchases goods with mixed dirhams that later become demonetized (kasād) before the seller receives payment?
A) The sale is automatically void
B) The seller has the option to cancel the sale
C) The buyer must replace the dirhams with valid currency
D) The sale remains valid and the seller must accept the demonetized coins

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh protects the seller by granting an option to rescind when the currency used in a sale becomes universally demonetized before payment, without automatically voiding the contract.

Ruling on Selling Mixed Dirhams with Their Own Kind with Disparity
Explanation of ruling on mixed coin exchange with disparity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling mixed dirhams with their own kind with disparity and identify the condition of possession in the session.

Core Idea
Mixed dirhams are treated as two types, so disparity does not make the sale riba — but qabd fi al-majlis is required.

When dealing with coins that are not pure silver or gold, the rules of exchange become nuanced. In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hanafi school on selling mixed coins (where the alloy predominates) with their own kind, even if the quantities differ. The passage explains why this is permissible and under what condition, and also notes an exception for certain coin types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling mixed dirhams with their own kind with disparity in count or weight. The passage states that this sale is valid (sahih).
2
Note the required condition
The condition for validity is possession in the session (qabd fi al-majlis), because this is considered an exchange of two different types (silver and alloy), similar to sarf.
3
Understand the rationale
The mixed coin is treated as consisting of two different substances: silver and copper (or other alloy). The alloy from one coin offsets the silver in the other, making the disparity permissible.
4
Recognize the exception
Some later scholars from Khorasan disallowed disparity for dalali and atarifa coins because they were the most precious and common in their lands, fearing it would lead to riba.
5
Note the melting scenario
If the silver burns away upon melting, the coin is treated as pure copper, and disparity is not allowed.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains that selling mixed coins (where the alloy predominates) with their own kind is valid even with disparity in quantity or weight, because the alloy in each coin is treated as a different substance (like copper) that is being exchanged for the silver in the other. Thus it is a valid exchange of two different categories. The condition of possession in the session (qabd fi al-majlis) is required because it is like sarf (currency exchange). The commentary also clarifies that if the silver in mixed coins burns away completely upon melting, leaving only copper, then it is treated as pure copper and disparity is not allowed.

Key Terms
دراهم مغشوشة (darahim maghshushah): Coins where the alloy (base metal) predominates over the silver or gold content.
صرف (sarf): A contract of exchanging currency (gold, silver, or money) for currency.
قبض في المجالس (qabd fi al-majalis): Taking physical possession of the exchanged items in the contract session before the parties separate.

Key Takeaway
The sale of mixed dirhams with disparity is valid only if the parties take possession in the session, because the mixture is treated as two different substances.

Effect of Kasad on Sale with Mixed Dirhams or Fulūs
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kasad's effect on sale validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of kasad on a sale and identify the thamarah al-khilaf for each.

Core Idea
Kasad of the price before delivery voids the sale according to Abu Hanifa but not according to the Sahibayn.

This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a sale where the currency used as price (mixed dirhams or fulus) becomes out of circulation (kasad) before delivery. The core issue is whether the attribute of being a price (thamaniyyah) is a conventional quality that lapses with kasad or a temporary impediment. The ruling varies among the founders, leading to different practical outcomes regarding compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is a sale where the price consists of mixed dirhams or fulus that later become out of circulation (kasad) before delivery. The dispute: does the sale become void or remain valid?
Focus on the timing: kasad before delivery.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds the sale becomes void (batil) because thamaniyyah is established by convention (istilah); when kasad occurs, the coins revert to being mere commodities, and the contract lacks a valid price.
Proof: the price is essential; without it, the sale is void.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the sale remains valid because the convention existed at the time of contract; kasad is a temporary impediment that does not void the contract, similar to a temporary market disruption.
They analogize to buying with dates that later become unavailable.
4
Distinguish between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on valuation
If the sale is valid but delivery is impossible, compensation is due. Abu Yusuf values the coins on the day of sale; Muhammad values them on the day of kasad (last day of circulation).
This affects the amount the buyer must pay.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer returns the commodity (or its value) as the sale is void. Under the Sahibayn, the buyer must pay the value of the coins at the specified date.
The fatwa differs among later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٤]قوله : فإن كسدت ; حد الكسادِ أن تترك المعاملة بها في جميع البلاد، وإن كان يروج في بعض البلاد، لا يبطل البيع، لكنّه يتعيَّبُ إذا لم تروج في بلدهم، فيتخيرُ البائع
كتاب الصرف تعريفه وأحكامه
بطل) : أي كسدت الفلوس قبل تسلمها بطل عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما : لا يبطل"، فعند أبي يوسف له يَحِبُّ قيمتها يوم البيع ، وعند محمد ه آخر ما يتعامل به الناس.
[١) قوله : بطل ; لأن الثمنية يثبت لها لعارض الاصطلاح، فإذا كسدت رجعت إلى أصلها، ولم تبق ثمناً ، فيبطل البيعُ لبقائه بلا ثمن، ويجب على المشتري رد المبيع إن كان قائماً ، ومثله أو قيمته إن كان هالكا ، كما في البيع لا الفاسد. [٢] قوله : لا يبطل ; لأنَّ العقد قد صح لبقاء الاصطلاح على الثمنية عـنـد وجودِه ، وإِنَّما تعدَّرَ التسليم بعده بالكساد، وذلك لا يوجب الفساد ; لاحتمال الزوالِ بالرواج، فصار كما لو اشترى ثياباً بالرطب ، ثم انقطع عن أيدي الناس، وإذا لم يبطل البيع عندهما وقد تعذر تسليمه تجب قيمته. لكن يعتبر قيمته يوم البيع عند أبي يوسف ; لأنَّ الثمن صارَ مضموناً به : كالمغصوب، فإنه يعتبر قيمته ; لأنه مضمون به، وعند محمد له يعتبر قيمته يوم الكساد، وهو آخرُ ما يتعاملُ النَّاسُ بها ; لأنه يوم الانتقال إلى القيمة ; لأنَّ المسمّى كان واجب التسليم إلى أن ينقطع ، فإذا انقطع انتقل إلى القيمة; للتعذر، فيعتبر قيمته يومئذ، وقال في «الذخيرة»: الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف له، وفي «المحيط»: يفتى بقول محمد .
And if he bought a commodity with mixed dirhams or with current fulus, it is valid. Then if they became out of circulation (kasad): [Footnote 4] He said: 'if they became obsolete': the definition of kasad is that transactions with them are abandoned in all regions. And if they circulate in some regions, the sale does not become void, but it becomes defective if they do not circulate in their region, so the seller has an option. Book of Sarf: its definition and rulings. They said: 'void': meaning the fulus became out of circulation before delivery, it becomes void according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it does not become void. According to Abu Yusuf, he must pay their value on the day of sale, and according to Muhammad, the last value they were transacted with. [Footnote 1] He said: 'void': because the status of being a price (thamaniyyah) is established due to convention, so when they become obsolete, they revert to their original substance and are no longer a price, so the sale becomes void as it remains without a price, and the buyer must return the commodity if it still exists, or its like or value if destroyed, as in a void (batil) sale. [Footnote 2] He said: 'does not become void': because the contract was valid due to the continuation of the convention of being a price at the time of existence, and afterward delivery became impossible due to obsolescence, and that does not cause voidness because there is possibility of reviving by circulation again. It is like buying clothes with dates and then the dates disappear from people's hands. When the sale does not become void according to them, and delivery is impossible, its value must be paid. But its value is considered on the day of sale according to Abu Yusuf because the price becomes like a guarantee, similar to usurped property. According to Muhammad, its value is considered on the day of obsolescence, which is the last day people transacted with it, because that is the day of transition to value. In al-Dhakhirah, the fatwa is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf, and in al-Muhit, the fatwa is on the opinion of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of kasad (coins going out of circulation) on a sale where the price is mixed dirhams or fulus. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that the sale becomes void (batil) because the coins lose their status as thaman (price) by convention, leaving the sale without a price. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the sale remains valid because the convention existed at the time of contract, and the subsequent kasad is like a temporary impediment. However, since delivery is impossible, compensation is due: Abu Yusuf values the coins on the day of sale, Muhammad on the day of kasad. The footnotes further clarify that according to Abu Hanifa, if the sold commodity is still in existence, it must be returned; if destroyed, the like or value is paid. The commentary also notes that the fatwa differs among later scholars.

Key Terms
كساد (kasad): The condition where coins or fulus are no longer accepted in transactions in all regions (according to Muhammad) or in the specific region (according to Sahibayn).
ثمنية (thamaniyyah): The legal status of being a price (thaman) in a contract, which is established by convention (istilah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The sale becomes void and the buyer must return the commodity or its value
B) The sale remains valid and the buyer must pay the value of the fulus on the day of kasad
C) The sale remains valid and the buyer must pay the value of the fulus on the day of sale
D) The sale becomes void but no compensation is due

Key Takeaway
The effect of kasad on a sale depends on whether the price is considered a conventional attribute that can be lost; the Hanafi school has multiple valid positions.

8 min
Loan of Fulūs That Become Out of Circulation
Explanation of a loan ruling with an intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on repaying borrowed fulus when they become out of circulation and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute over the definition of kasad.

Core Idea
When borrowed currency dies, the borrower owes its like or value, but 'out of circulation' splits the school.

This lesson explores a practical question: if you borrow fulus (copper coins) and they become demonetized before you repay, what must you return? The Hanafi school agrees on the basic duty to repay the like (mithl) or its value, but disagrees on what 'out of circulation' means. This intra-madhhab dispute illustrates how differing usul principles (considering local vs. universal custom) produce different rulings. We will unpack the positions of Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn, and see how this relates to a parallel dispute on selling fulus for two fulus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
State the basic obligation when borrowed fulus become out of circulation: the borrower must repay the like (mithl) of the fulus, or their value at the lender's option.
2
Define 'Out of Circulation' (Kasad)
Understand that the text distinguishes between complete disappearance (inqita') — not found in the market — and the broader concept of kasad (non-circulation). The dispute centres on the scope of kasad.
3
Analyse Imam Muhammad's Position
Imam Muhammad holds that kasad requires the fulus to be out of circulation in all regions (jami' al-buldan). This is based on his view that the validity of a sale of fulus depends on the convention of all people (istilah al-kull).
4
Analyse the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that kasad in the specific region of the contract suffices. This follows from their view that local convention (istilah al-ba'd) is sufficient for certain transactions.
5
Connect to the Parallel Dispute
Note that this difference is rooted in the dispute over selling fulus for two fulus (a currency exchange with delay). The Sahibayn allow it based on local custom; Imam Muhammad requires universal custom.

(ولو استقرض فلوساً فكسدت يَحِبُّ مثلُها ) إن شاء أخذه وإن شاء أخذ قيمته، وحد الانقطاع أن لا يوجد في السوق، وإن كان يوجد في يد الصيارفة وفي البيوت. وفي «الفتاوى الصغرى»: حد الكسادِ أن لا تروج في جميع البلدان، ثم قال : هذا قول محمد ه ، وأما عندهما الكساد في بلده يكفي لفساد البيع في تلك البلدة بناءً على اختلافهم في بيع الفلوس بالفلسين، فعندهما يجوز اعتبارا لاصطلاح بعض ، وعنده : لا يجوز ; لأنه يعتبر اصطلاح الكل. كذا في «الرمز»). (And if he borrowed fulus and they became out of circulation, he must pay their like) He may take them if he wishes, or take their value. The definition of complete disappearance (inqita') is that they are not found in the market, even if they exist with moneychangers and in houses. In al-Fatawa al-Sughra: the definition of kasad is that they do not circulate in all regions. Then he said: This is the opinion of Muhammad. As for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), according to them, kasad in one's region suffices for the invalidity of the sale in that region, based on their difference in selling fulus for two fulus. According to them, it is permissible considering the convention of some; according to him (Abu Hanifa), it is not permissible because he considers the convention of all. So in al-Ramz. Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage deals with a loan (qard) of fulus (copper coins). If the fulus become out of circulation (kasad) before repayment, the borrower must repay the like (mithl) in kind or value, at the lender's option. The definition of complete unavailability (inqita') is that the coins are not found in the market. There is also a discussion about the definition of kasad: according to Muhammad, it requires all regions; according to the Sahibayn, it suffices if the coins are out of circulation in the relevant region. This is analogous to the dispute on selling fulus for two fulus. The footnote clarifies that the obligation to pay 'mithl' applies if the borrowed fulus are destroyed; if they still exist, the ruling is different (possibly returning the same coins).

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A loan contract where the lender gives ownership of fungible items to the borrower, who must repay the like (mithl) in quantity and type.
مثل (mithl): The equivalent in kind, quantity, and quality; for fulus, it means similar coins in circulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The exact same coins he borrowed
B) The like (mithl) of the fulus, or their value at the option of the lender
C) Nothing, because the contract becomes void
D) The value of the fulus at the time of the contract only

Key Takeaway
In a loan of currency, if the currency becomes out of circulation, the borrower must repay the like (mithl) or its value; the definition of 'out of circulation' is disputed within the Hanafi school.

The Effect of Currency Obsolescence on a Sale: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on voidness of sale due to flus obsolescence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on a sale where the currency becomes obsolete before delivery and explain the key difference in their valuation.

Core Idea
Currency status (thamaniyya) is by custom; its loss does not automatically void a contract made when the custom prevailed.

This passage examines a specific scenario: a sale concluded with flus (copper coins) as the price, but before the buyer takes delivery, the coins are withdrawn from circulation and become worthless. The Hanafi school disagrees on the validity of the sale. Abu Hanifa rules it void; his two disciples hold it valid. Their disagreement extends to the measure of compensation when delivery becomes impossible. Understanding this dispute reveals important Hanafi principles about the role of custom ('urf) in determining the nature of money and the point at which a contract is evaluated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The scenario is a sale with flus as the price, but the flus become obsolete before delivery. The question is whether the sale is void or valid, and what compensation is due.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds the sale void because thamaniyya (being a price) is based on custom; when custom ceases, the flus revert to commodity, so the contract lacks a valid price.
Abu Hanifa's ruling is that the sale is batil (void).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the sale valid because the contract was valid at formation when the custom was intact. Subsequent obsolescence is a temporary impediment that does not invalidate the contract.
They analogize to buying clothes with dates that later become unavailable.
4
Distinguish between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on valuation
If the sale is valid and delivery is impossible, compensation is required. Abu Yusuf values on the day of sale (like a usurped item); Muhammad values on the day of obsolescence (last day of circulation).
Both agree the sale is valid but differ on the point of valuation.
5
Note the fatwa divergence and practical consequence
Later sources differ: al-Dhakhira gives fatwa on Abu Yusuf; al-Muhit gives fatwa on Muhammad. Thus no single mufta bihi exists.
The practical consequence depends on which opinion is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطل) : أي كسدت الفلوس قبل تسلمها بطل عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما : لا يبطل"، فعند أبي يوسف له يَحِبُّ قيمتها يوم البيع ، وعند محمد ه آخر ما يتعامل به الناس. [1) قوله : بطل ؛ لأن الثمنية يثبت لها لعارض الاصطلاح، فإذا كسدت رجعت إلى أصلها، ولم تبق ثمناً ، فيبطل البيعُ لبقائه بلا ثمن، ويجب على المشتري رد المبيع إن كان قائماً ، ومثله أو قيمته إن كان هالكا ، كما في البيع لا الفاسد. [٢] قوله : لا يبطل ؛ لأنَّ العقد قد صح لبقاء الاصطلاح على الثمنية عـنـد وجودِه ، وإِنَّما تعدَّرَ التسليم بعده بالكساد، وذلك لا يوجب الفساد ؛ لاحتمال الزوالِ بالرواج، فصار كما لو اشترى ثياباً بالرطب ، ثم انقطع عن أيدي الناس، وإذا لم يبطل البيع عندهما وقد تعذر تسليمه تجب قيمته. لكن يعتبر قيمته يوم البيع عند أبي يوسف ؛ لأنَّ الثمن صارَ مضموناً به : كالمغصوب، فإنه يعتبر قيمته ؛ لأنه مضمون به، وعند محمد له يعتبر قيمته يوم الكساد، وهو آخرُ ما يتعاملُ النَّاسُ بها ؛ لأنه يوم الانتقال إلى القيمة ؛ لأنَّ المسمّى كان واجب التسليم إلى أن ينقطع ، فإذا انقطع انتقل إلى القيمة؛ للتعذر، فيعتبر قيمته يومئذ، وقال في «الذخيرة»: الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف له، وفي «المحيط»: يفتى بقول محمد .
(It becomes void) meaning: if the flus become obsolete before receipt, it becomes void according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it does not become void. So according to Abu Yusuf, its value on the day of sale is obligatory, and according to Muhammad, the last value with which people transact. [1] His statement: 'it becomes void' because thamaniyya (currency status) is established for it by virtue of custom (istilah), so if it becomes obsolete, it returns to its original state and is no longer a price, so the sale becomes void because it remains without a price, and it is obligatory for the buyer to return the sold item if it still exists, or its equivalent or value if it is destroyed, as in a void sale... [2] His statement: 'it does not become void' because the contract was valid due to the continuation of the custom of it being a currency at the time of the contract, and delivery became impossible after that due to obsolescence, and that does not necessitate invalidity because of the possibility of its removal by re-circulation, so it is like if someone bought clothes with dates and then dates became unavailable from people's hands. And if the sale is not void according to the two, and delivery is impossible, its value becomes obligatory. But its value on the day of sale is considered according to Abu Yusuf because the price became guaranteed like a usurped item, and according to Muhammad, its value on the day of obsolescence is considered, which is the last day people transact with it... and in al-Dhakhira: the fatwa is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf, and in al-Muhit: fatwa is given on the opinion of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale where the currency (flus) becomes obsolete before the buyer takes delivery. Abu Hanifa holds that the sale becomes void because the currency status (thamaniyya) is based on custom; when the flus cease to be used as currency, they revert to mere commodity, so the sale lacks a valid price. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the sale remains valid because the custom of treating them as currency was present at the time of contract; the subsequent obsolescence is a temporary impediment to delivery, not a defect in the contract. They analogize to buying clothes with dates that later become unavailable. However, they differ on the obligatory compensation: Abu Yusuf says value at the day of sale, while Muhammad says value at the day of obsolescence (the last day of circulation). The text then notes that fatwa differs: al-Dhakhira prefers Abu Yusuf, al-Muhit prefers Muhammad. This shows that the ruling is not settled in the later school.

Key Terms
فُلُوس (fulūs): Copper coins used as currency by custom (istilah)
كَسَاد (kasād): Obsolescence of coins, i.e., removal from circulation
ثَمَنِيَّة (thamaniyya): Quality of being a price/currency

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the sale become void when flus become obsolete?
A) Because the flus no longer exist.
B) Because the thamaniyya (currency status) is based on custom and is lost with obsolescence.
C) Because the buyer did not take delivery.
D) Because the seller committed fraud.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's treatment of flus obsolescence reveals a key principle: the nature of currency ('urf) determines its legal effect, and a contract valid at formation may survive temporary impediments to delivery.

Returning Borrowed Flus That Become Obsolete: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Valuation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on obligation when borrowed flus become obsolete

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the obligatory payment when borrowed flus become obsolete and are destroyed, and explain the practical consequence of each.

Core Idea
Borrower returns like of obsolete flus if coins exist; if destroyed, Abu Hanifa insists on mithl, Sahibayn require value at different dates.

When a borrower receives flus (coins) that later become obsolete, what must be returned? If the coins still exist, the answer is the same coins. But if they are destroyed, the Hanafi masters differ. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores the dispute, linking it to the definition of obsolescence and the nature of flus as mithli or qimi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its condition
The passage begins with the ruling: if borrowed flus become obsolete, the like (mithl) is obligatory. But note: this applies only if the coins are destroyed; if they exist, the same coins are returned. Distinguish between existing and destroyed flus.
2
Define obsolescence and its scope
The text gives two definitions: Muhammad requires obsolescence in all towns; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) accept obsolescence in the town alone, based on their view that custom of some suffices in the sale of flus. This disagreement affects when the ruling applies.
The pronoun 'عندهما' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; 'عنده' refers to Abu Hanifa.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation
If the borrowed flus are destroyed, Abu Hanifa says mithl (like) is obligatory; Abu Yusuf says value at the day of receipt; Muhammad says value at the day of obsolescence. This is the core dispute.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is the amount the borrower must pay. Different dates yield different values. The fatwa authority is not specified here, so all three views are transmitted within the school.
Earlier in the sale context, fatwa differed, but this passage does not settle it.

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on borrowing flus that later become obsolete. The borrower has the option to return the same coins if they are still in existence, or their value if they are destroyed. The definition of obsolescence is that the coins are not found in the market, even if still present with individuals. There is a dispute about the scope of obsolescence: Muhammad holds it must be in all towns, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? The text then notes that if the borrowed flus are destroyed, the like is obligatory according to Abu Hanifa because flus are considered a standard commodity (mithli). Abu Yusuf obligates the value at the time of receipt, while Muhammad obligates the value at the time of obsolescence. This echoes the earlier dispute in the sale context.

Key Terms

مثلي (mithlī): Fungible good that can be replaced by similar items
كساد (kasād): Obsolescence; loss of circulation as currency
