Ruling on Bay' al-'Inah (Double Sale)
Explaining the ruling and definitions of bay' al-'inah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on bay' al-'inah, distinguish between its forms, and apply the ruling based on whether the original asset returns to the seller.

Core Idea
If the same item returns to the seller, bay' al-'inah is prohibitively disliked; if not, it is merely contrary to preference.

Bay' al-'inah is a controversial transaction that involves selling an item on credit and then buying it back for cash. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, the proofs from hadith, and the key distinction—whether the asset returns to the seller—that determines the level of dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction structure
Describe the basic form of bay' al-'inah: a person buys goods on credit, then sells them for cash to the same seller or a third party, often as a device to obtain cash without explicit riba.
The text gives multiple forms: direct buyback, tripartite arrangement, and scenarios involving agents.
2
Locate the Hanafi distinction
Understand when it is makruh tahriman vs. khilaf al-awla: if any part of what the seller originally parted with returns to him, it is makruh tahriman; otherwise, merely contrary to better practice.
This distinction is from Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir.
3
Examine the evidence
Analyze the hadith cited: 'If you engage in bay' al-'inah and follow the tails of camels, you will be humiliated and your enemy will overcome you.' Note its chain is weak but the meaning supports prohibition.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud and Bayhaqi; it indicates disapproval.
4
Review the inter-madhhab difference
Compare Hanafi (disliked if asset returns) with Shafi'i (permissible without stipulation). Note the practical consequence: Shafi'is allow it in more cases.
The difference is rooted in whether the intention to circumvent riba is considered a defect.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a case (e.g., buyer resells to a third party, not the seller), decide the ruling: if the original item does not return to the seller, it is khilaf al-awla, not makruh tahriman.
Practice with examples from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفيل أمرَهُ أصيله بأن يتعين عليه ثوبا ففعل، فهو له) : أي أمر الأصيل الكفيل بأن يشتري ثوباً بطريق العينة قوله : بطريق العينة ; قال العلامة الفيومي في «المصباح المنير : العينة : بالكسر السلف، واعتان الرجلُ اشترى الشيء بنسيئة، وبعثه عيناً بعين: أي حاضراً، وعين التاجر تعيناً، والاسم العينة : بالكسر ، وفسرها الفقهاء : بأن يبيع الرجلُ متاعه إلى أجل ثم يشتريه بثمن حال ليسلم به من الربا. وقيل لهذا البيع عينة ; لأن مشتري السلعة إلى أجل يأخذ بدلها عيناً : أي نقداً ; وذلك حرام إذا اشترط المشتري على البائع أن يشتريها منه بثمن معلوم، فإن لم يكن بينهما شرط فأجازها الشافعي الله له لوقوع العقد سالماً عن المفسدات، ومنعها بعض المتقدمين، وكان يقول : هي أخت الربا. فلو باعها المشتري من غير بائعها في المجلس، فهي منه أيضاً، لكنها جائزة بالاتفاق. انتهى. وقال الفقهاء : بيع العينة : هو أن يقول له اشتر من الناس حريراً أو غيره من الأنواع ثم بعه، فما ربح البائع منك وخسرته أنت فعلي. وصورته : أن يأتي هو إلى تاجر فيطلب منه القرض، ويطلب التاجر الربح، ويخاف من الربا، فيبيعه التاجر ثوباً يساوي عشرة مثلاً بخمسة عشر نسيئة ; ليبيعه هو بالسوق ليصل إلى العشرة، ويجب عليه للبائع خمسة عشر إلى أجل، أو يقرضه خمسة عشر درهماً ثم يبيعه المقرض ثوباً يساوي عشرة بخمسة عشر. فيأخذ الدراهم التي أقرضه على أنها ثمن الثوب، فيبقى عليه خمسة عشر درهماً ، فإذا فعل ذلك نفذ عليه ، والربح الذي ربحه التاجر يلزمه، ولا يلزم الأمر شيئاً من ذلك ; لأنه إما ضامن لما يخسره نظرا إلى قوله : علي، والضمان بالخسران لا يجوز ; لأن الضمان لا يكون إلا بمضمون وإمّا توكيله بالشراء نظراً إلى الآمر به، فلا يجوز أيضاً لجهالة الحرير وثمنه. وقيل صورته : أن يشتريه بأكثر من قيمته ليبيعه بأقل من ذلك الثمن بغير البائع ، ثم يشتريه البائع من ذلك الغير بالأقل الذي اشتراه به ، ويدفع ذلك الأقل إلى بائعه، فيدفعه بائعه إلى المشتري المديون تحرُّزاً عن شراء ما باع بأقل مما باع قبل نقد الثمن. ومنهم من صور بغير ذلك. وهذا مكروه مذموم شرعاً ; لما فيه من الإعراض عن مبرة الإقراض، والبخل المذموم ، رُوِيَ أن محمداً الله قال : هذا البيع في قلبي كأمثال الجبال ذميم اخترعه أكلةُ الربا، وقد ذمهم الشارع عليه، فقال : «إذا تبايعتم بالعينة واتبعتم أذناب الإبل ذللتهم وظهر عليكم عدوكم ) : أي إذا اشتغلتم بالحرث عن الجهاد. وفي رواية: «سلّط عليكم شراركم فيدعوا خياركم فلا يستجاب لكم وقيل : وإياك والعينة فإنها لعينة، وقال أبو يوسف : هذا البيع غير مكروه; لأنه فعله كثير من الصحابة وحمدوا عليه، ذكره في «الفتح»). ثم قال ما حاصله : إن الذي يقع في قلبي أنه إن فعلت صورة يعود فيها إلى البائع جميع ما أخرجه أو بعضه كعود الثوب إليه في الصورة المارة، وكعود الخمسة في صورة إقراض الخمسة عشر، فيكره يعني تحريماً. فإن لم يعد كما إذا باعه المديون في السوق فلا كراهية فيه، بل خلاف الأولى، فإن الأجل قابله قسط من الثمن والقرض غير واجب عليه دائماً، بل هو مندوب وما لم يرجع إليه العين التي خرجت منه لا يسمى بيع العينة ; لأنه من العين المسترجعة لا العين مطلقاً، وإلا فكل بيع بيع العينة. انتهى. هذا وإن طال الكلام لكن لا يخلو عن كشف المرام ، وهاهنا مباحث نفيسة تركناها لغرابة المقام، وإن شئت فارجع إلى مبسوط وطات الفقهاء الكرام، والتوفيق من الملك العلام.
The guarantor, whose principal ordered him to buy a garment for him by way of 'inah, and he did so, then it belongs to him. Meaning: the principal ordered the guarantor to buy a garment by way of 'inah. His statement: 'by way of 'inah'. Allamah Fayyumi said in al-Misbah al-Munir: Al-'inah (with kasrah) means a loan. A man's i'tana means he bought something on credit. 'He sold it eye to eye' means for cash. The trader practiced 'ayana, and the noun is 'inah (with kasrah). The jurists interpret it as: a man sells his goods on credit and then buys them back for cash to escape riba. This sale is called 'inah because the buyer of the goods on credit takes their cash equivalent. This is haram if the buyer stipulates that the seller will buy them back for a known price. If there is no stipulation, al-Shafi'i permitted it because the contract is free from invalidating factors. Some early scholars prohibited it, saying it is the sister of riba. If the buyer sells it to someone other than the seller in the same session, it is also from it [i.e., 'inah] but is unanimously permissible. End. The jurists said: Bay' al-'inah is that he says to him, 'Buy from people silk or other items then sell it, so whatever the seller profits from you and you lose is on me.' Its form: He goes to a merchant, asks for a loan, and the merchant wants profit but fears riba, so the merchant sells him a cloth worth ten for fifteen on credit so that he can sell it in the market to get ten, and he owes the merchant fifteen on credit, or he lends him fifteen dirhams then the lender sells him a cloth worth ten for fifteen, so he takes the dirhams he lent as the price of the cloth, leaving him owing fifteen dirhams. If he does this, it is binding on him, and the profit the merchant made is owed by him, and the principal is not liable for anything because either he is a guarantor for losses according to his saying 'on me', and guaranteeing against loss is not permissible because guarantee is only for what is guaranteed, or he is his agent in buying according to the one ordering, so it is also not permissible due to ignorance of the silk and its price. Some said its form: He buys it for more than its value to sell it for less than that price to someone other than the seller, then the seller buys it from that other for the lower price he sold it for, and pays that lower amount to his seller, so his seller pays it to the debtor buyer to avoid buying what he sold for less before paying the price. Some depicted it differently. This is disliked and blameworthy in Sharia because it involves turning away from the virtue of lending and blameworthy stinginess. It is reported that Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: 'This sale in my heart is like mountains, despicable, invented by the consumers of riba, and the Lawgiver condemned them.' He said: 'If you engage in bay' al-'inah and follow the tails of camels, you will be humiliated and your enemy will overcome you' meaning if you occupy yourselves with agriculture over jihad. In another narration: 'Your worst ones will be given authority over you, and your best ones will pray but not be answered.' It is said: 'Beware of 'inah for it is accursed.' Abu Yusuf said: This sale is not disliked because many Companions did it and were praised for it, as mentioned in al-Fath. Then he (Ibn al-Humam) said, summarized: What occurs in my heart is that if one does a form in which everything he expended returns to the seller or part of it, like the cloth returning in the previous form, and like the return of five in the form of lending fifteen, then it is disliked, meaning tahriman (prohibitively disliked). If it does not return, as when the debtor sells it in the market, then there is no dislike in it, rather it is contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla), because the term is met with a part of the price and the loan is not always obligatory upon him, rather it is commendable. As long as the item that left him does not return to him, it is not called bay' al-'inah, because it is from the returned item, not the item absolutely; otherwise, every sale would be bay' al-'inah. End. This, even if lengthy, does not fail to disclose the sought. There are valuable discussions here which we left due to the oddity of the context. If you wish, refer to the extended writings of the noble jurists. Success is from the All-Knowing King.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage from the book of kafalah (guarantee) in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses a specific scenario where a principal orders his guarantor to buy a garment by way of 'inah (a form of double sale) and then discusses the ruling of bay' al-'inah in detail. The passage type is an expansion of a ruling with proofs and inter-madhhab differences. The commentary begins by quoting al-Misbah al-Munir defining 'inah as a loan or a sale on credit. The jurists define bay' al-'inah as selling goods on credit and then buying them back for cash to avoid the appearance of riba. The text explains that this transaction is haram if there is a stipulation to buy back, but if there is no stipulation, Imam al-Shafi'i permits it, while some early Hanafis prohibit it as 'the sister of riba'. The passage then presents the Hanafi jurists' definition and multiple classical forms of 'inah, including a tripartite arrangement where a merchant sells a cloth for more than its value on credit, the debtor sells it for cash to a third party, and the original merchant buys it back at the lower price. The text states that all these forms are makruh (disliked) and blameworthy because they turn away from virtuous lending and exhibit stinginess. A hadith is cited warning against 'inah and following cow tails (i.e., neglecting jihad for agriculture). Abu Yusuf's opinion is mentioned that it is not disliked because many Companions practiced it. However, the relied-upon position, as given by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir, distinguishes: if any of the seller's original item returns to him (the same cloth or the same money), it is makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked); if it does not return, there is no dislike beyond khilaf al-awla (contrary to preference). The Hanafi madhhab thus carefully differentiates between forms of 'inah that involve a clear circumvention of riba and those that do not. The passage concludes by noting that the discussion is lengthy but necessary, and further details can be found in larger works.

Key Terms
بيع العينة (bay' al-'inah): A sale where a person buys goods on credit and then sells them for cash to the same seller or another party as a device to obtain cash without explicit riba.
العينة (al-'inah): Linguistically: a loan. Technically: a sale involving credit and immediate cash to circumvent riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this text, when is bay' al-'inah considered makruh tahriman?
A) When the buyer sells the item in the market to a third party.
B) When the same item (or its value) returns to the seller.
C) Whenever a sale on credit followed by a cash sale occurs.
D) When the buyer does not pay immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on bay' al-'inah depends crucially on whether the original asset returns to the seller; if it does, the transaction is prohibitively disliked as a disguised form of riba.

Ruling on Bay' al-'Inah and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Discusses definition, ruling, and intra-Hanafi dispute on Bay' al-'Inah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Bay' al-'Inah, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Ibn al-Humam, and apply Ibn al-Humam's distinction to determine whether a given transaction is makruh tahriman or khilaf al-awla.

Core Idea
Bay' al-'Inah is makruh tahriman only if the same property returns to the seller; otherwise it is khilaf al-awla.

Bay' al-'Inah is a credit sale structure that some use to obtain cash while avoiding explicit riba. However, whether it is permissible is disputed even within the Hanafi school. This lesson unpacks the definition, the primary hadith warning, Abu Yusuf's counter-argument from companion practice, and Ibn al-Humam's decisive distinction that limits prohibition to cases where the seller re-acquires the same property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of Bay' al-'Inah
Read the passage carefully: it involves a seller buying back the same item at a lower price to provide cash to the buyer. Note the key element: return of the same property (العين) to the seller.
The root word 'ayn (عين) refers to the specific property that goes back to the seller.
2
Extract the two positions within the Hanafi school
Locate the quote from Abu Yusuf (not makruh) and the general view that it is 'disliked and blameworthy'. Then find Ibn al-Humam's detailed position which splits the ruling based on whether the property returns.
Abu Yusuf's position is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Humam's distinction
Ibn al-Humam says: if all or part of what the seller gave returns to him (e.g., the cloth or the excess money), it is makruh tahriman. If not (the buyer sells to a third party in the market), it is only khilaf al-awla.
This distinction resolves the apparent conflict between the hadith warning and the companion practice.
4
Identify the evidence used: hadith and companion practice
The passage cites a hadith about 'inah leading to humiliation. Abu Yusuf relies on the fact that many companions did it and were praised. Understand the weight of each dalil.
The hadith chain is weak according to Ibn Hajar, but it is still cited as a warning.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
Based on the tarjih from Ibn al-Humam, the relied-upon position in later Hanafi works is: makruh tahriman when the property returns, otherwise khilaf al-awla.
This is a classical example of a nuanced fatwa that balances evidence.

Then the seller buys it from that other person at the lower price he bought it for, and pays that lower price to his seller, so his seller pays it to the debtor buyer to avoid selling what he sold for less before payment of the price. Among them are those who picture it differently. This is disliked and blameworthy in Shari'ah, because it involves turning away from the virtue of lending and blameworthy miserliness. It is narrated that Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'This sale is in my heart like mountains, detestable; it was invented by the eaters of riba, and the Lawgiver condemned them.' He said: 'If you engage in 'inah sales and follow the tails of cattle, you will be humiliated and your enemy will overcome you' – meaning when you occupy yourselves with cultivation instead of jihad. In another narration: 'Your worst people will be given power over you, so your best people will pray but not be answered.' And it is said: 'Beware of al-'inah, for it is accursed.' Abu Yusuf said: 'This sale is not disliked, because many Companions did it and were praised for it,' as mentioned in al-Fath. Then he (Ibn al-Humam) said, the summary of which is: 'What occurs in my heart is that if a form is done in which all or some of what he gave returns to the seller, like the return of the cloth in the previous picture, and like the return of the five in the picture of lending fifteen, then it is disliked, meaning tahriman. If it does not return, as when the debtor sells it in the market, there is no dislike in it, rather it is khilaf al-awla, because the term is met with a portion of the price, and lending is not obligatory upon him always, but rather recommended, and as long as the property that left him does not return to him, it is not called Bay' al-'Inah, because it is from the property taken back, not property absolutely; otherwise, every sale would be Bay' al-'Inah.' End. This, though lengthy, does not fail to unveil the goal. (1) In Sunan Abi Dawud (3:274) and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (5:316); Ibn Hajar said in al-Dirayah (2:151): 'Its chain is weak, and it has another better chain in Ahmad.' (2) Fath al-Qadir (6:324). (3) I.e., Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir (6:324).

Commentary
This passage discusses Bay' al-'Inah, a credit sale where one avoids direct lending by buying and selling an item at a markup. The author first presents the view that it is makruh (disliked) and blameworthy, citing a hadith warning against it. Then Abu Yusuf's opposing view is mentioned: he considers it not makruh because many Companions engaged in it. Finally, Ibn al-Humam from Fath al-Qadir provides a nuanced ruling: if the seller takes back the same property (e.g., cloth or money), it is makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked); if not, it is only khilaf al-awla (contrary to what is better). Thus, the text highlights an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this issue.

Key Terms
بيع العينة (Bay' al-'Inah): A sale where a buyer purchases an item on credit at a higher price, then sells it for cash to get money, avoiding direct riba.
العينة (al-'Inah): Derived from 'ayn (property), referring to turning away from debt to property.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahriman): Disliked to the level of prohibition; closer to haram than tanzihan.

A) Always, regardless of circumstances
B) When the buyer resells the item on the market
C) When the property (item or money) returns to the original seller
D) Only if the sale involves interest

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on Bay' al-'Inah hinges on whether the same property returns to the seller; if it does, it is makruh tahriman, otherwise it is merely khilaf al-awla.

Definition of Bay' al-'Ayna and Ruling on Profit in Void Agency
Defines bay' al-'ayna and derives the ruling on profit in void agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define bay' al-'ayna, explain why the agency in the described kafalah scenario is void, and state who bears the profit/loss.

Core Idea
Bay' al-'ayna uses inflating price to circumvent riba; the resulting agency is void, leaving the surety liable.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary defines a problematic sale known as bay' al-'ayna and examines a scenario where a principal instructs his surety to buy a garment at an inflated price. The analysis reveals that the agency is void due to uncertainty, and consequently the profit (actually a loss) falls on the surety. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the boundaries of valid agency and the prohibition of riba circumvention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction structure
Recognize that bay' al-'ayna involves seeking cash by buying a commodity at an inflated price on credit. Note the key elements: a principal, a surety (kafil), a garment, and an inflated price.
The passage explains that 'ayna is derived from 'ayn (cash) because it turns away from debt to cash.
2
Recognize the void agency
Understand why the agency (wakalah) is void: the garment type is unspecified (among many types) and the price is unknown because the excess over market value is uncertain. This is wakalah fasidah.
Uncertainty (jahl) in either the subject matter or the price renders the agency void in Hanafi law.
3
Determine the ruling on profit/loss
Since the agency is void, the surety acts on his own behalf. The 'profit' from selling the garment (the difference between purchase price and sale price) is actually a loss that the surety must bear. The principal's guarantee to cover that loss is invalid.
The phrase 'whatever profit its seller makes is upon him' means the surety bears the loss.
4
Understand the refinement from Fath al-Qadir
The commentator clarifies that the scenario is not about the principal asking for a loan, but about directing the surety to buy the garment. This refines the definition of bay' al-'ayna.
This subtle point distinguishes between seeking a loan and entering a sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع العينة : أن يستقرض رجل من تاجر شيئاً، ولا يقرضُهُ قرضاً حسناً، بل يعطيه عيناً، ويبعها من المستقرض بأكثر من القيمة ، فالعينة مشتقة من العين سمي بها ؛ لأنه إعراض عن الدين إلى العين، فالأصيل أمر كفيله بأن يشتري ثوباً بأكثر من القيمة ليقضي به دينَهُ ففعل، فالثَّوبُ للكفيل ؛ لأن هذه وكالة فاسدة" لعدم تعين النُّوبِ والثَّمن.
(وما ربح بائعُهُ فعليه) : أي إذا اشترى الثَّوبَ بخمسة عشر، وهو يساوي عشرة، فباعه بالعشرة، فالرِّبْحُ الذي حصل للبائع، وهو الخمسة التي صارت على الكفيل، فعلى الكفيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكالة لما لم تصح صارَ كأَنه قال له : إن اشتريت ثوبا بشيء، ثُمَّ بعتَهُ بأقل من ذلك فأنا ضامن لذلك الخسران، فهذا الضمان ليس بشيء.
[1] قوله : أن يستقرض ...الخ ؛ قال في «فتح القدير ) (۱): وأما تفسيره بأن يستقرض من تاجر...الخ، فلا يصح هذا إذ ليس المراد من قوله : تعين علـي حـريـرا اذهب فاستقرض، فإن لم يرض المسؤول أن يقرضك فاشتر منه الحرير بأكثر من قيمته، بل المقصود اذهب فاشتر على هذا الوجه. انتهى. وهذا بحث دقيق فانظر فيه. [٢] قوله : وكالة فاسدة ؛ أما كونه وكالة ؛ فلأنه أمره بالشراء علـى وجـه مخصوص، وأما فسادها ؛ فلجهالة الثوب، فإن الثوب أجناس مختلفة ولم يعين نوعاً منه ؛ ولجهالة الثمن فإن بيع العينة لا يتحقق إلا بازدياد الثمن من قيمة السلعة، وتلك الزيادة مجهولة، فإن كان الدين معلوماً وقدر الدين مع تلك الزيادة ثمن السلعة فيكون الثمن مجهولا.
And the sale of 'ayna is that a man seeks a loan from a merchant, and he does not give him a good loan, but rather gives him 'ayn (cash) and sells it to the borrower for more than its value. So 'ayna is derived from 'ayn; it is named thus because it is a turning away from debt to cash. So the principal ordered his kafil to buy a garment for more than its value to pay off his debt, and he did so. So the garment is for the kafil because this is a void agency due to the lack of specification of the garment and the price. (And whatever profit its seller makes is upon him): that is, if he bought the garment for fifteen while it is worth ten, then sold it for ten, the profit obtained by the seller, which is the five that became upon the kafil, is upon the kafil because since the agency was invalid, it is as if he said to him: If you buy a garment for something, then sell it for less than that, I am guarantor for that loss. So this guarantee is nothing. [1] His saying: 'that he seeks a loan... etc.'; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'And as for its interpretation as seeking a loan from a merchant... etc., this is not correct because the intended meaning of his saying: 'Find me silk, go and seek a loan, if the one asked does not agree to lend you, then buy from him the silk for more than its value' but rather the intent is go and buy in this manner.' End. And this is a subtle investigation, so look into it. [2] His saying: 'void agency'; as for it being an agency, because he ordered him to purchase in a specific manner; and as for its voidness, due to the ignorance of the garment, for the garment has different types and he did not specify a type; and due to the ignorance of the price, because bay' al-'ayna is not realized except by the increase of the price over the value of the commodity, and that increase is unknown, so if the debt is known and the amount of the debt along with that increase is the price of the commodity, then the price is unknown.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from the sharh defines bay' al-'ayna and explains the ruling on profit in a specific scenario involving kafalah. The author clarifies that in bay' al-'ayna, the principal instructs his surety (kafil) to buy a garment at an inflated price to obtain cash to pay a debt. This is considered a void agency due to uncertainty in the garment type and the excessive price. Consequently, the garment belongs to the kafil, not the principal. Regarding the profit, if the kafil buys the garment for 15 (above market value of 10) and later sells it for 10, the loss of 5 is considered a profit for the seller (the kafil) but it is actually a loss that the kafil must bear because he acted on his own. The surety agreement to cover such loss is invalid. The commentary further refines the definition by citing Fath al-Qadir, noting that the scenario is not about seeking a loan but about buying on that basis. It also explains why the agency is void: uncertainty in the garment (unspecified type among many) and uncertainty in the price (the excess over value is unknown).

Key Terms
بيع العينة (bay' al-'ayna): A transaction where a person seeks cash from a merchant by buying a commodity at an inflated price on credit to get immediate cash.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one party guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
وكالة فاسدة (wakalah fasidah): A void agency due to uncertainty in the subject matter or price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Both A and B.
D) Because the kafil was not present.

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-'ayna creates a void agency due to uncertainty; any resulting profit or loss falls on the agent (kafil), not the principal.

Testimony in Sale Documents: Admission vs. Report
Distinguishes when testimony is admission vs mere report

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a witness's testimony in a sale document constitutes an admission (iqrar) barring a later ownership claim, and when it is merely a report (khabar) that does not bar such a claim.

Core Idea
Absolute testimony admits ownership; testimony on parties' admission is just a report.

A witness who writes testimony in a sale document may later claim that the property was actually his. The Hanafi school teaches that the effect depends on the wording of the testimony. If the witness asserts the seller's ownership absolutely, he cannot later contradict himself. But if he merely reports what the parties admitted, he remains free to claim ownership. This lesson unpacks that distinction through the principle that a rational person intends his actions to be valid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base ruling
Identify that mere writing and sealing of testimony does not automatically invalidate a later ownership claim. The passage begins by stating that writing and sealing alone are not enough to bar the claim.
Note: sealing was customary to prevent forgery, not to confirm ownership.
2
Distinguish absolute testimony from admission-based testimony
Focus on the key condition: if the testimony is absolute (e.g., 'he sold his property' or 'an outright effective sale'), it amounts to an admission of the seller's ownership, making a later claim contradictory and invalid. If the testimony is based on the parties' admission, it is merely a report and does not create a contradiction.
The phrase 'shahada 'ala iqrarihim' is critical.
3
Apply the rational principle
The proof (dalil) is the principle 'al-‘āqil yurīdu bi-tasarrufihi al-ṣiḥḥa' — a rational person intends his actions to be valid. When a witness writes absolute testimony, he intends a valid transaction implying the seller's ownership; thus his later denial is inconsistent.
This is an istihsan based on custom and rationality.
4
Identify the legal consequence
If the testimony is absolute, the later claim is invalid (batil). If the testimony is based on the parties' admission, the later claim is valid (sahih). The passage also notes that testifying before a judge (formal shahada) has the same effect as absolute writing.
Formal judicial testimony also binds the witness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ شَهِدَ وَخَتَمَ لَا. وَإِنَّمَا قَالَ وَخَتَمَ لِأَنَّ الْمَعْهُودَ كَانَ الْخَتْمَ صِيَانَةً عَنِ التَّغْيِيرِ وَالتَّبْدِيلِ. لَا تَبْطُلُ دَعْوَاهُ بِمُجَرَّدِ الْكِتَابَةِ وَالْخَتْمِ إِلَّا إِذَا شَهِدَ عِنْدَ الْحَاكِمِ وَقَضَى بِشَهَادَتِهِ أَوْ لَمْ يَقْضِ فَيَكُونُ تَسْلِيمًا. قَالُوا: إِنْ كُتِبَ فِي الصَّكِّ بَاعَ مِلْكَهُ أَوْ بَيْعًا بَاتًا نَافِذًا، وَهُوَ كَتَبَ شَهِدَ بِذَلِكَ بَطَلَتْ دَعْوَاهُ؛ لِأَنَّ شَهَادَتَهُ تَكُونُ إِقْرَارًا بِأَنَّ الْبَائِعَ مَالِكٌ، فَإِذَا ادَّعَى الْمِلْكَ لِنَفْسِهِ يَكُونُ مُنَاقِضًا. وَلَوْ كَتَبَ شَهَادَتَهُ عَلَى إِقْرَارِ الْعَاقِدَيْنِ لَا تَبْطُلُ؛ لِعَدَمِ التَّنَاقُضِ، فَإِنَّهُ مُجَرَّدُ إِخْبَارٍ. وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الْعَاقِلَ يُرِيدُ بِتَصَرُّفِهِ الصِّحَّةَ.
And if he testified and sealed, it does not (invalidate his claim). He said 'and sealed' because the custom was that sealing was for protection against alteration and substitution. His claim is not invalidated by mere writing and sealing unless he testified before a judge and the judge ruled by his testimony or did not rule, then it becomes an admission. They said: If in the document it is written 'he sold his property' or 'an outright effective sale,' and he wrote 'I testify to that,' his claim is invalidated because his testimony becomes an admission that the seller was the owner, so if he claims ownership for himself it is contradictory. And if he wrote his testimony based on the admission of the two contracting parties, it is not invalidated because there is no contradiction, for it is merely a report. That is because a rational person intends validity in his actions.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from a Hanafi sharh. It explains when a witness's testimony in a sale document constitutes an admission (iqrar) of the seller's ownership, thereby barring the witness from later claiming ownership. The matn says 'and if he testified and sealed, it does not'—meaning his later claim is not automatically invalidated. The commentary explains that sealing was customary to prevent forgery, not to confirm ownership. However, if the witness testifies before a judge, even without a verdict, it becomes binding admission. The key distinction: if the witness writes an absolute testimony like 'he sold his property' or 'an outright effective sale,' he admits the seller's ownership, so his later claim is contradictory and invalid. But if he writes testimony based on the parties' admission (iqrar al-āqidayn), he merely reports what they said, so no contradiction arises. The reasoning relies on the principle that a rational person intends his actions to be valid (al-‘āqil yurīdu bi-tasarrufihi al-ṣiḥḥa), so testifying to a sale implies the seller's ownership unless qualified.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahāda): Testimony written in a document regarding a sale, which may be absolute or based on the parties' admission.
إقرار (iqrār): Admission of a right or ownership; considered binding and creates a legal presumption.
بيع بات (bay' bātt): An outright, irrevocable sale without condition or option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His claim is accepted because writing testimony is not an admission.
B) His claim is rejected because his testimony amounts to an admission of the seller's ownership.
C) His claim is rejected only if he sealed the document.
D) His claim is accepted if he did not testify before a judge.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between absolute testimony (admission) and testimony on parties' admission (report) determines whether a witness can later claim ownership.

Contradictory Testimony and Ownership Claims
Commentary on contradictory testimony in sale claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on contradictory testimony in sale claims and apply the principle of iqrar to determine when a subsequent ownership claim is void.

Core Idea
A contradictory claim after written testimony is void because the testimony is an admission that binds the witness.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling on contradictory testimony in sale deeds. The classical Hanafi position treats a witness's written statement as an admission (iqrar) if it explicitly states that the seller sold his own property. If the witness later claims ownership, his claim is void due to contradiction. However, if the witness merely reports the parties' own confession, no contradiction arises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating claims in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, identify the witness's act: writing a testimony in a sale deed. Then determine the ruling: if the witness later claims ownership for himself, his claim is void (batil).
The ruling is based on the principle of contradiction (tanaqud).
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof here is not a textual source but a reasoning (talil): the written testimony constitutes an admission (iqrar) by the witness that the seller owned the property. Iqrar is legally binding.
The proof is the legal implication of iqrar, not an explicit Quranic verse or hadith.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key is the phrase 'sold his own property' (باع ملكه) or 'final binding sale' (بيعاً باتاً نافذاً). These words show the witness acknowledged the seller's ownership and valid sale. Claiming later that the property is his own contradicts this admission.
The contradiction nullifies the later claim.
4
Distinguish the exception
If the witness writes based on the parties' confession (إقرار العاقدين), his later claim is not void because he is merely reporting what others said, not making an admission of his own.
The key is whether the testimony reflects the witness's own knowledge or a report of others' statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باع ملكه أو بيعاً باتاً نافذاً، وهو كتب شهد بذلك بطلت : أي بطلت دعواه بعد أن شهد هذه الشهادة ; لأن شهادته تكون إقراراً بأن البائع قد باع ملكه، أو باع بيعاً باتاً نافذاً، فإذا ادعى الملك لنفسه يكون مناقضاً. ولو كتب شهادته على إقرار العاقدين لا : أي لا تبطل دعواه بعد هذه الكتابة ; لعدم التناقض. وهو قوله : بيعاً باتاً نافذاً ; فإن المراد به اللفظ دون المعنى. وقوله : لعدم التناقض ; فإنه لا يكون تسليماً إذ لا يتعلق به حكم، وإنما هو مجرد إخبار، ولو أخبر أن فلاناً باع شيئاً كان له أن يدعيه.
He sold his property or a final binding sale, and if he wrote that he testified to that, his claim is void: meaning his claim is void after he gave this testimony, because his testimony constitutes an admission that the seller sold his property, or sold a final binding sale, so if he claims ownership for himself, he is contradictory. And if he wrote his testimony based on the parties' confession, [the claim] is not void: meaning his claim is not void after this writing due to lack of contradiction. And his statement: 'a final binding sale' means the word is taken literally, not the meaning. And his statement: 'due to lack of contradiction' means it is not considered an admission because no legal ruling attaches to it; it is merely a report, and if he reports that someone sold something, he can still claim it.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of contradictory testimony in sales. The commentator explains that if a witness writes in a deed that the seller sold his own property (ملكه) or that the sale was final and binding (باتاً نافذاً), then later claims that the property actually belongs to him, his claim is void. This is because his written testimony is considered an admission (إقرار) that the seller owned the property and validly sold it. Claiming ownership afterwards is a legal contradiction (مناقضة). However, if the witness writes his testimony based on the parties' own confession (إقرار العاقدين) rather than his own knowledge, then his later claim of ownership is not void. The reason is that in this case, he is merely reporting what the parties said; no legal ruling of admission attaches to his writing. The commentator further clarifies that the phrase 'a final binding sale' (بيعاً باتاً نافذاً) refers to the literal wording, not the underlying meaning, to emphasize that the contradiction arises from the explicit statement that the seller sold his property.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony given by a witness, here in the context of a written deed (sakk).
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession; a legal declaration against one's own interest.
تناقض (tanaqud): Contradiction in statements or positions, rendering later assertions legally invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His claim is valid because writing is not as strong as oral testimony.
B) His claim is void due to contradiction with his earlier admission.
C) His claim is void only if he also testified orally.
D) His claim is valid because he can change his testimony.

Key Takeaway
A witness's written testimony that explicitly affirms the seller's ownership bars the witness from later claiming ownership for himself, due to the binding nature of admission and prohibition of contradiction.

The Invalidity of Kafālat al-Khalāṣ: Abu Hanifa vs the Two Disciples
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafālat al-khalāṣ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the two disciples' positions on kafālat al-khalāṣ, explain the basis of each, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A guarantee to free a reclaimed item is invalid unless it includes an alternative to return the price.

The passage begins by introducing a specific type of kafālah: a guarantee to extricate a sold item (e.g., a slave) if it is reclaimed by a third party. The Hanafi school is divided on its validity. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute, the underlying interpretations, and the practical resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its form
Locate the phrase 'ḍamān al-khalāṣ' (كفالة الخلاص). Understand that it is a guarantee to free the sold item and deliver the exact item to the buyer if reclaimed.
The key is that the guarantor promises to secure the physical item itself, not monetary compensation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and reason
Abu Hanifa rules the guarantee invalid (bāṭil) because the guarantor may lack the ability to free the item—e.g., if the claimant refuses or the slave is free. Committing to an impossible act is invalid.
The principle: farāgh al-dhimmah (original freedom from liability) is not overridden by doubt.
3
State the disciples' ruling and reinterpretation
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider such a guarantee valid, interpreting it as a guarantee of defect (ḍamān al-darak)—i.e., an obligation to return the price if the item is not freed.
They read the guarantee as implicitly including an alternative, making it feasible.
4
Identify the resolution and practical consequence
The passage clarifies that if the guarantee is phrased as 'extricate the item or return the price,' it is valid by agreement. The mufta bihi is that a bare guarantee of extrication is invalid (Abu Hanifa's view).
This shows that the ikhtilaf turns on interpretation of the wording.

Chapter of Kafalah: Guarantee, and the guarantee of two men, and the guarantee of a slave.
Or the muḍārib guarantees the price to the owner of the capital, or the agent for sale guarantees to his principal.
That is, if one guarantees liberation [of the sold item], it is not valid according to Abū Ḥanīfah — that he stipulates that if the sold item is claimed, he will extricate it and deliver the very item to him by any means. This is invalid because he has no ability for this.
According to them (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), it is valid, and it is interpreted as a guarantee of defect (ḍamān al-darak).
(Or the muḍārib guarantees the price to the owner of the capital): that is, the muḍārib sold and guaranteed the price to the owner.

Because the original state is freedom of liability (farāgh al-dhimmah), so obligation is not established by doubt and probability. Thus they said.
[1] His statement: According to Abū Ḥanīfah, the disagreement is based on its interpretation. They (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) interpreted it as extricating the sold item if possible, and returning the price if not possible — this is essentially a guarantee of defect. The Imām (Abū Ḥanīfah) interpreted it as mere extrication of the item, and this is beyond his ability. As in al-Nahr. If one guarantees to extricate the item or return the price, it is valid because he guaranteed what he is capable of fulfilling — that is, delivering the item if the claimant permits, and returning the price if not. [3] His statement: it is not permissible... etc., the summary thereof: for the non-permissibility of kafālah in these two cases, there are two [reasons]:
One of them: The price is a trust in the hands of the muḍārib and the agent, so they are trustees, and a trustee does not become a guarantor — so the guarantee is a change of the ruling of the Law, and a [free] person cannot be enslaved, so the guarantee is rejected for each of them, like stipulating it upon a depositee or borrower.
The second: Kafālah is an undertaking of demand, and the right of demand belongs to the muḍārib and the agent. The right of receipt belongs to the agent and the muḍārib by virtue of being principal in the sale; for this reason the agency is invalidated by the death of the principal, the death of the owner of capital, and by his dismissal. And if the principal or the owner of capital appointed [someone else] for receipt of the debt and then dismissed him, it is valid.

Similarly, if the owner of capital appointed the muḍārib for receipt of the price for him, then dismissed him, [it is valid]; because he is the contracting party, so rights revert to him, and the contracting party for another in terms of rights is like the contracting party for himself; for this reason the demand is exclusive to him. If the buyer swore that the principal has no claim against him, he is truthful in his oath; if he swore that the agent has no claim against him, he breaks his oath. So when it is established that the agent is principal in receipt, if he guarantees, he becomes a guarantor for himself, so it is not valid.

Indeed, if he paid his partner's share without prior guarantee, it is valid, and he does not have recourse for what he paid, unlike the case of guarantee, where he does have recourse for what he paid because he discharged a corrupted [debt]. For example, two men have a debt against a man, and one of them guarantees for his partner the share of the debt — his guarantee is not valid. And if one of them voluntarily pays the partner's share of the debt, it is permissible.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāyah discusses several types of kafālah (suretyship/guarantee) that are invalid. It begins with the case of 'ḍamān al-khalāṣ' — a guarantee to free a sold item (e.g., a slave) if it is reclaimed. According to Imam Abū Ḥanīfah, such a guarantee is invalid because it requires an impossible action: the guarantor may have no power to free the slave (e.g., if the purchaser does not consent or if the slave is actually free). The two disciples (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) differ, interpreting such a guarantee as an ordinary 'ḍamān al-darak' (guarantee against defect) — which is valid. The author clarifies that if the guarantee is phrased as 'extricate the item or return the price,' it is valid because it is within the guarantor's capacity.

The second case: a muḍārib (silent partner) or a wakīl (agent) guaranteeing the sale price to the principal. This is invalid for two reasons: (1) The price in their hands is an amāna (trust), and a trustee cannot become a guarantor — doing so would change the Shariah's ruling. (2) The right to demand payment belongs to the muḍārib/wakīl, not the principal; thus, if they guarantee, they are guaranteeing for themselves, which is meaningless. The text explains that the muḍārib/wakīl is considered the principal (aṣīl) in receiving payment, so a guarantee by them amounts to a self-guarantee.

The third case: one of two co-sellers guarantees his partner's share of the sale price in a single transaction. This is also invalid because each seller is a trustee of the price for the other? Actually, the rationale is similar: the price is a trust? The text states it is invalid because the price is a trust and the right of demand belongs to each seller? The author then notes exceptions: a messenger or an agent for specific purposes (like selling spoils or marriage) can validly give a guarantee because they are mere intermediaries. Finally, examples of shared debts and inheritance are given to illustrate the general principle: a partner cannot guarantee another partner's share of a debt held jointly against a third party, though voluntary payment without prior guarantee is valid.

Key Terms
كفالة الخلاص (kafālat al-khalāṣ): A guarantee to free a sold item (e.g., a slave) if it is reclaimed by a third party, so that the buyer receives the exact item.
ضمان الدرك (ḍamān al-darak): A guarantee of defect, i.e., to compensate the buyer if the sale proves defective (e.g., if the item is reclaimed, the guarantor returns the price).
فراغ الذمة (farāgh al-dhimmah): The original state of a person's legal responsibility being free from any obligations or debts; a presumption of freedom from liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the guarantor may not be a reliable person.
B) Because the guarantor may lack the ability to free the item, making it an impossible commitment.
C) Because the buyer does not consent to the guarantee.
D) Because the guarantee is not witnessed.

Key Takeaway
Kafālat al-khalāṣ is invalid unless it includes an alternative obligation (like returning the price) that is within the guarantor's capacity.

intermediate

Why Trustees Cannot Guarantee the Sale Price
Ruling on invalidity of guarantee by trustees with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons why a guarantee by a muḍārib or wakīl of the sale price is invalid according to the Hanafi school, and apply the reasoning to co-sellers and joint debts.

Core Idea
A trustee cannot become a guarantor for the entrusted property because it changes the ruling of the Sharīʿah.

The passage continues with two more cases of invalid kafālah: a muḍārib (silent partner) or wakīl (agent) guaranteeing the sale price to the principal, and a co-seller guaranteeing the partner's share. This lesson unpacks the two reasons for invalidity—the trust nature of the property and the self-guarantee issue—and extends the principle to similar situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases
Locate the cases: muḍārib guarantees the price to the capital owner; wakīl guarantees to his principal; one co-seller guarantees the partner's share of the sale price.
All involve a trustee (amīn) who holds the price as a trust (amāna).
2
Analyze the first reason: trust and change of ruling
The price is a trust in the hands of the muḍārib/wakīl. A trustee cannot become a guarantor because that would change the ruling (ḥukm) of the Sharīʿah—trusteeship implies no liability for loss, while guarantee creates liability.
Like a depositee cannot guarantee the deposit.
3
Analyze the second reason: self-guarantee
The right to demand payment belongs to the muḍārib/wakīl as they are the contracting parties. If they guarantee, they become guarantors for themselves—a nullity.
They are considered principals (aṣīl) in the sale transaction.
4
Apply to co-sellers and joint debts
Similarly, one partner cannot guarantee another's share of a joint debt because each is a trustee of the debt for the other, and the right of demand is shared.
Voluntary payment without prior guarantee is valid and does not create recourse.

وعندهما يصح، وهو محمول على ضمان الدَّرَك.

(أو الوكيل بالبيع لموكله ) : أي باغ الوكيل وضَمِنَ للموكل الثَّمن، وإنما لا يجوز قلنا إنمـا لـم يصرف إليه ؛ لأن فراغ الذمة أصل فلا يثبت الشغل بالشك والاحتمال. هكذا قالوا.
[١] قوله : عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ والخلاف مبني على تفسيره ، فهمـا فــراه : بتخليص المبيع إن قدر عليه ورد الثمن إن لم يقدر عليه ، وهذا ضمانُ الدَّركِ في المعنى، وفسره الامام : بتخليص المبيع فقط ولا قدرة له. كذا في «النهر». ولو ضمن تخليص المبيع أو ردَّ الثمن جاز ؛ لأنه ضَمِنَ ما يملك الوفاء به، وهو تسليم المبيع إن أجاز المستحق المبيع ورد الثمن إن لم يجز، كذا في «الرمز». [٢]قوله : إذ لا قدرة له على هذا ؛ لأنه إن ظهر مستحقاً فـ نا فربما لا يساعده المستحق، أو حُرَّاً فلا يقدر مطلقاً، والتزام ما لا يقدر على الوفاء به باطل، كذا في «الكفاية».

وكما أن الرجل إذا مات وله دين على رجل وترك ابنين، فكفل أحدهما لأخيه عن المديون بحصة أخيه لا تصحُ الكفالة، ولو تبرَّعَ أحدُهما فأدَّى حصة صاحبه من
Chapter of Kafalah: Guarantee, and the guarantee of two men, and the guarantee of a slave.
Or the muḍārib guarantees the price to the owner of the capital, or the agent for sale guarantees to his principal.
That is, if one guarantees liberation [of the sold item], it is not valid according to Abū Ḥanīfah — that he stipulates that if the sold item is claimed, he will extricate it and deliver the very item to him by any means. This is invalid because he has no ability for this.
According to them (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), it is valid, and it is interpreted as a guarantee of defect (ḍamān al-darak).
(Or the muḍārib guarantees the price to the owner of the capital): that is, the muḍārib sold and guaranteed the price to the owner.
(Or the agent for sale guarantees to his principal): that is, the agent sold and guaranteed the price to the principal. But it is not permissible; why it is not permissible? Because the original state is freedom of liability (farāgh al-dhimmah), so obligation is not established by doubt and probability. Thus they said.
[1] His statement: According to Abū Ḥanīfah, the disagreement is based on its interpretation. They (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) interpreted it as extricating the sold item if possible, and returning the price if not possible — this is essentially a guarantee of defect. The Imām (Abū Ḥanīfah) interpreted it as mere extrication of the item, and this is beyond his ability. As in al-Nahr. If one guarantees to extricate the item or return the price, it is valid because he guaranteed what he is capable of fulfilling — that is, delivering the item if the claimant permits, and returning the price if not. As in al-Ramz.
[2] His statement: because he has no ability for this; because if the item proves to be claimed, then perhaps the claimant will not cooperate, or [the slave] becomes free, and he cannot [enslave him] at all — committing to what one cannot fulfill is invalid. As in al-Kifāyah.
Two aspects:
[3] His statement: it is not permissible... etc., the summary thereof: for the non-permissibility of kafālah in these two cases, there are two [reasons]:
One of them: The price is a trust in the hands of the muḍārib and the agent, so they are trustees, and a trustee does not become a guarantor — so the guarantee is a change of the ruling of the Law, and a [free] person cannot be enslaved, so the guarantee is rejected for each of them, like stipulating it upon a depositee or borrower.
The second: Kafālah is an undertaking of demand, and the right of demand belongs to the muḍārib and the agent. The right of receipt belongs to the agent and the muḍārib by virtue of being principal in the sale; for this reason the agency is invalidated by the death of the principal, the death of the owner of capital, and by his dismissal. And if the principal or the owner of capital appointed [someone else] for receipt of the debt and then dismissed him, it is valid.
Or one of the two sellers [guarantees] the share of his partner from the price of a slave they both sold in a single transaction ... because the price is a trust with the muḍārib and the agent, so the guarantee is a change of the ruling of the Law.
... and because the right of demand belongs to the muḍārib and the agent, so they become guarantors for themselves.
(Or one of the two sellers "the share of his partner from the price of a slave they both sold in a single transaction"
... his dismissal; because the price is owed to the agent or the muḍārib from the buyer, since the rights of the contract revert to the contracting party.
Similarly, if the owner of capital appointed the muḍārib for receipt of the price for him, then dismissed him, [it is valid]; because he is the contracting party, so rights revert to him, and the contracting party for another in terms of rights is like the contracting party for himself; for this reason the demand is exclusive to him. If the buyer swore that the principal has no claim against him, he is truthful in his oath; if he swore that the agent has no claim against him, he breaks his oath. So when it is established that the agent is principal in receipt, if he guarantees, he becomes a guarantor for himself, so it is not valid.
[1] His statement: they become guarantors; contrary to a messenger and an agent for selling spoils from the Imam and an agent for marriage, where their guarantee is valid; because each of them is an intermediary and transmitter, such that if the commander forbids them from receiving the substitute, his prohibition is valid.
[2] His statement: or one of the two sellers... etc.; its image: two men sold a jointly owned slave to a man in one transaction, and one of them guaranteed to his partner his share of the price — it is not valid. Indeed, if he paid his partner's share without prior guarantee, it is valid, and he does not have recourse for what he paid, unlike the case of guarantee, where he does have recourse for what he paid because he discharged a corrupted [debt]. As mentioned in Radd al-Muḥtār, quoting from Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn. Similarly, kafālah for each partner for a shared debt is invalid absolutely, even if by inheritance. For example, two men have a debt against a man, and one of them guarantees for his partner the share of the debt — his guarantee is not valid. And if one of them voluntarily pays the partner's share of the debt, it is permissible.
Similarly, if a man dies and has a debt against a man, leaving two sons, and one of them guarantees for his brother regarding the debtor for his brother's share — the guarantee is not valid. And if one of them voluntarily pays the brother's share from
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāyah discusses several types of kafālah (suretyship/guarantee) that are invalid. It begins with the case of 'ḍamān al-khalāṣ' — a guarantee to free a sold item (e.g., a slave) if it is reclaimed. According to Imam Abū Ḥanīfah, such a guarantee is invalid because it requires an impossible action: the guarantor may have no power to free the slave (e.g., if the purchaser does not consent or if the slave is actually free). The two disciples (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) differ, interpreting such a guarantee as an ordinary 'ḍamān al-darak' (guarantee against defect) — which is valid. The author clarifies that if the guarantee is phrased as 'extricate the item or return the price,' it is valid because it is within the guarantor's capacity.

The second case: a muḍārib (silent partner) or a wakīl (agent) guaranteeing the sale price to the principal. This is invalid for two reasons: (1) The price in their hands is an amāna (trust), and a trustee cannot become a guarantor — doing so would change the Shariah's ruling. (2) The right to demand payment belongs to the muḍārib/wakīl, not the principal; thus, if they guarantee, they are guaranteeing for themselves, which is meaningless. The text explains that the muḍārib/wakīl is considered the principal (aṣīl) in receiving payment, so a guarantee by them amounts to a self-guarantee.

The third case: one of two co-sellers guarantees his partner's share of the sale price in a single transaction. This is also invalid because each seller is a trustee of the price for the other? Actually, the rationale is similar: the price is a trust? The text states it is invalid because the price is a trust and the right of demand belongs to each seller? The author then notes exceptions: a messenger or an agent for specific purposes (like selling spoils or marriage) can validly give a guarantee because they are mere intermediaries. Finally, examples of shared debts and inheritance are given to illustrate the general principle: a partner cannot guarantee another partner's share of a debt held jointly against a third party, though voluntary payment without prior guarantee is valid.

Key Terms
الأمين (al-amīn): A trustee who holds property but is not liable for loss unless negligence; includes a muḍārib, wakīl, depositee, etc.
فراغ الذمة (farāgh al-dhimmah): The original state of a person's legal responsibility being free from any obligations or debts; a presumption of freedom from liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muḍārib sells on behalf of the capital owner and then guarantees the price. What is the second reason for invalidity?
A) The muḍārib does not own the goods.
B) The right of demand belongs to the muḍārib, so he becomes a guarantor for himself.
C) The guarantee is not witnessed.
D) The capital owner did not authorize the sale.

Key Takeaway
A trustee (amīn) cannot guarantee the entrusted property because it would change the Sharīʿah's ruling and often involves self-guarantee.

Guarantee by a Co-Seller: One Contract vs. Two
Explains validity of guarantee with two vs. one contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guarantee by co-sellers in one vs. two contracts, explain the reasons (self-guarantee and premature division of debt), and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A guarantee by a co-seller is invalid if the debt is shared from a single contract, but valid when each seller has a separate contract.

In Hanafi sales law, when two people jointly sell an item, questions arise about whether one can guarantee the other's share of the price. This lesson examines the ruling from the matn of Wiqayah and its commentary, 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, clarifying when such a guarantee is valid and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Two sellers sell a slave. If they use one contract and one guarantees the other's share, the guarantee is invalid. If they use two separate contracts, it is valid.
The ruling is stated in the matn: 'With two transactions it is valid.'
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
With one contract, the sellers are partners in the debt. A guarantee would involve the guarantor guaranteeing himself (since he has a share) and would require dividing the debt before receipt, which is prohibited.
Division of debt before receipt (qismat al-dayn qabla qabdihi) is impermissible due to gharar.
3
Analyze the consequence of invalid division
If division were attempted, it leads to an infinite regress: each payment is shared, so the guarantor takes back half repeatedly, nullifying the arrangement.
The commentator references al-Tabyeen for this reasoning.
4
Distinguish the valid scenario
When sales are in two separate contracts, each seller's debt is exclusive to him. No partnership exists, so the guarantee is on a pure debt, not on oneself, and no division of debt is required.
The key is the absence of partnership (la shirka).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa requires repeating the verb 'I sold' for separate contracts; the Sahibayn consider detailing the price sufficient. The relied-upon position follows the Sahibayn.
The commentator cites al-Bahr for this dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبصفقتين صح : أي باعا عبداً صفقة واحدة ، وضَمِنَ أحدهما لصاحبه حصته من الثَّمن لا يصح؛ لأنه لو صح الضمانُ مع الشركة يصير ضامنا لنفسه، ولو صح في نصيب صاحبه يؤدي إلى قسمةِ الدَّين قبل قبضه ، وذا لا يجوز. بخلاف ما لو باعاه بصفقتين ، فإنَّه يصح الضمان ؛ لأنَّه لا شركة.
[١] قوله : بصفقتين صح ؛ صورته : أن رجلين باعَ كل واحدٍ منهما نصفه بعقد على حدة، ثم ضمن أحدهما لصاحبه حصته من الثمن صح الضمان.
[٢] قوله : يصير ضامناً ؛ إذ ما من جزء يؤديه المشتري أو الكفيل من الثمن الأول لشريكه فيه نصيب.
[٣] قوله : وذا لا يجوز؛ لأن القسمة عبارة عن الإفراز والحيازة، وهو أن يصير حق كل واحد منهما مفرزاً في حيز على حدة، فهو لا يتصور في غير العين ؛ لأن الفعل الحسي يستدعي محلاً حسياً، والدين حكمي، فلا يرد عليه الفصل الحسي. فإن لم تصح قسمته يكون كل شيءٍ يؤديه إلى شريكه مشتركاً بينهما، فيرجعُ المؤدي بنصف ما أدى لكونه مشتركاً بينهما، ثم يرجع أيضاً بنصف الباقي إلى أن لا يبقى في يده شيء، فيؤدي تجويزه ابتداءً إبطاله انتهاء، كذا في «التبيين».
[٤] قوله : بصفقتين ؛ قد اعتبروا هاهنا لتعددِ الصفقة تفصيل الثمن، وذكروا في البيوع أنما هذا قولهما، وأما على قوله فلا بُدّ من تكرار لفظ : بعت ، كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
[٥] قوله : لأنه لا شركة ؛ فإنّه إذا تعددت الصفقة فما وجب لكل واحد منهما بعقده يكون له خاصة بلا شركة.
And with two transactions it is valid: i.e., if two men sell a slave in one transaction and one guarantees the other's share of the price, it is not valid because if the guarantee were valid with partnership, he would become a guarantor for himself, and if valid for the partner's share, it would lead to division of debt before its receipt, which is not permissible. Unlike if they sold in two transactions, then the guarantee is valid because there is no partnership.
[1] The commentator's statement 'with two transactions it is valid': its form is that two men each sold his half with a separate contract, then one guaranteed the other's share of the price; the guarantee is valid.
[2] The statement 'he would become a guarantor': because every part that the buyer or guarantor pays from the first price, the partner has a share in it.
[3] The statement 'that is not permissible': because division is separation and appropriation, that each one's right becomes separate in a distinct space, which is not conceivable for non-physical entities because physical action requires a physical locus, and debt is legal, so physical separation cannot be applied to it. If its division is not valid, then everything he pays to his partner is shared between them, so the payer takes back half of what he paid because it is shared, then he also takes back half of the remainder until nothing remains in his hand, so allowing it initially leads to its nullification eventually, as in 'al-Tabyeen'.
[4] The commentator's statement 'with two transactions': they considered here that for multiple transactions, the price is detailed, and they mentioned in sales that this is their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) view, but according to him (Abu Hanifa) the word 'sold' must be repeated, as in 'al-Bahr' and others.
[5] The statement 'because there is no partnership': when the transaction is multiple, what is owed to each one by his contract is for him exclusively without partnership.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a guarantee (kafalah) when two sellers sell a slave jointly. If they sell with one contract and one seller guarantees the other's share of the price, it is invalid. The reason: such a guarantee would involve the guarantor guaranteeing himself (since they are partners in the debt), and it would lead to division of the debt before it is collected, which is prohibited. If they sell with two separate contracts, the guarantee is valid because there is no partnership. The commentator explains each point: the form of the valid case, the reason for invalidity (every payment involves the partner's share), the prohibition of dividing a debt before receipt, and the consequence of invalid division leading to an infinite regress. Also, the commentator notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it's enough that the two contracts are separate in terms of price details, while according to Abu Hanifa, the word 'sold' must be explicitly repeated. The relied-upon position is that the guarantee is valid only with separate contracts and detailed division of the price.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A transaction or sale contract.
قسمة الدين (qismat al-dayn): Division of a debt before its receipt, which is prohibited in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason a guarantee by a co-seller is invalid when the sale is in one contract?
A) The guarantor becomes liable for the entire debt.
B) The guarantor guarantees himself, and it leads to division of debt before receipt.
C) The buyer must agree to the guarantee.
D) The slave is not present.

Key Takeaway
A guarantee by a co-seller is valid only when the sales are in separate contracts, because only then is there no partnership in the debt.

Ruling on Guarantor of Liability (Damān al-Darak)
Ruling on timing of guarantor liability after claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the timing of recourse against the guarantor of liability (damān al-darak) and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
The guarantor of liability is only liable after the buyer's loss is finalized by a court decree, not upon mere claim.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in the Hanafi school regarding the guarantor of liability (damān al-darak) in a sale. The key issue is when the buyer can pursue the guarantor after a third party claims ownership of the purchased item. The Hanafi position in zahir al-riwaya is that mere claim does not invalidate the sale, so the guarantor's liability is conditional on a judicial decree against the seller. This lesson will clarify the reasoning and note an intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the factual scenario
Identify the parties: a buyer purchases an item, a third party guarantees the seller's liability (damān al-darak) in case the item is later claimed by a rightful owner. Then a claim (istihqāq) occurs.
2
Identify the Hanafi position on the effect of the claim
According to zahir al-riwaya, the sale is not invalidated merely by the claim. The buyer cannot immediately recourse against the guarantor.
The sale remains valid until the buyer rescinds or the court decrees the return of the price.
3
Analyze the rationale
The reasoning: the claim does not nullify the inception of the sale (the initial contract was valid), so it should not nullify its continuation. The guarantor's liability is linked to the seller's obligation to return the price, which only arises after a decree.
The text uses an aqli (rational) argument: 'since it does not nullify the beginning, it does not nullify the continuation.'
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (in al-Amali) holds that the sale is immediately invalidated upon the claim, implying immediate liability of the guarantor. This is a legitimate difference within the school.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is the one in zahir al-riwaya.
5
Apply the ruling
Conclusion: The buyer cannot take the guarantor until the court decrees that the seller must return the price. The guarantor's liability is conditional on the buyer's loss being finalized.
This prevents double compensation and ensures legal clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يؤخذ ضامنُ الدَّرَك إن استحق المبيعَ ما لم يقض بثمنه على بائعه ، إذ بمجرَّدِ الاستحقاق لا ينتقض البيع في ظاهر الرواية
وله : في ظاهر الرواية ؛ وفي رواية «الأمالي»: عن أبي يوسف له : إنه يبطل
قوله : لا ينتقض البيع ؛ لأن الاستحقاق لا ينفي ابتداء البيع، فالأولى أن لا ينفي البقاء.
صورة المسألة : أنه إذا اشترى أمةٌ وكَفِـل لـه رجـل بالدرك فاستحقت ، لم يأخذ المشتري الكفيل حتى يقضى لـه بالـثمـن عـلـى البائع بالاستحقاق، وهو لم يخرج عن ملكه ما لم يَفْسَخ البيع.
ويَحْكُمْ على البائع برد الثمن على المشتري، وبمجرد الاستحقاق لا ينفسخ ؛ ولهذا لو أجاز المستحق البيع قبل الفسخ جاز ، فلو كان منتقضاً لما جاز، فإذا لم ينتقض لم يجب الثمن على البائع ، ولم يخرج عن ملكه ؛ لأن بدل المستحق مملوك.
ألا ترى أنه لو كان ثمنها عبداً فأعتقه البائع في هذه الحالة عُتق ، وكذا لو كان المشتري باع الجارية من إنسان فاستحقت من يد الثاني ليس للمشتري الأول أن يرجع على بائعه ما لم يُقضَ عليه بالثمن للثاني ؛ كيلا يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد. فإذا حكم الحاكم بالثمن عليه انتقض وسقط احتمال الإجازة، ولزم البائع ردَّ الثمن، فيلزم كفيله ضرورة بخلافِ القضاء بالحرمة ؛ لأنَّ البيع يبطل بها لعدم المحلية فيرجع على البائع والكفيل
And the guarantor of liability (damān al-darak) is not taken if the sold item is claimed (by a third party) unless the price has been decreed against the seller. Because merely by the claim of ownership, the sale is not invalidated in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).
And it is said: in the apparent narration; and in the narration of al-Amali from Abu Yusuf that it is invalidated.
His statement: The sale is not invalidated because the claim does not nullify the inception of the sale, so it is more appropriate that it does not nullify its continuation.
The scenario: That if someone buys a slave-girl and a man guarantees liability (darak) for him, and she is claimed (by a third party), the buyer does not take the guarantor until the price is decreed against the seller due to the claim, and he (the seller) has not removed it from his ownership unless the sale is rescinded.
And the judge decrees the seller to return the price to the buyer, and by mere claim the sale is not rescinded; therefore, if the claimant validates the sale before rescission, it is valid. If it were invalidated, it would not be valid. So when it is not invalidated, the price is not incumbent on the seller, and it does not leave his ownership because the substitute for the claimed item is owned.
Do you not see that if its price was a slave and the seller freed him in this state, the emancipation is valid. Similarly, if the buyer sold the slave-girl to someone and she is claimed from the second, the first buyer cannot recourse against his seller until the price is decreed against him for the second; to avoid two compensations coming into one ownership. So when the judge decrees the price against him, the sale is invalidated and the possibility of validation falls, and the seller is bound to return the price, so his guarantor is necessarily bound. Contrary to a decree of prohibition (haram), because the sale is void due to lack of legal capacity, so he recourses against the seller and the guarantor.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on the guarantor of the seller's liability (damān al-darak) in a sale. The scenario: A buyer purchases an item, and a third party (the guarantor) guarantees that if the item is later claimed by a rightful owner, the guarantor will compensate. The text rules that the guarantor is not pursued by the buyer until the court has decreed that the seller must return the price. This is because merely the claim of ownership (istihqāq) does not automatically invalidate the sale in the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwaya). The sale remains valid until the buyer rescinds or the court decrees. Therefore, the seller is not yet obligated to return the price, and the guarantor's liability has not accrued. The reasoning is that the claim does not nullify the inception of the sale, so it should not nullify its continuation. Examples are given: if the buyer sells the item to a third person and then it is claimed, the first buyer cannot recourse against his seller until he himself is decreed to pay the price to the second buyer, to avoid double compensation. Narration from Abu Yusuf (in al-Amali) holds that the sale is immediately invalidated, but the relied-upon view is the one in zahir al-riwaya (the six books of Imam Muhammad).

Key Terms
ضامن الدرك (damān al-darak): A guarantee of the seller's liability in case the sold item is later found to be owned by another.
استحقاق (istihqāq): A claim of ownership by a third party that the sold item rightfully belongs to them.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known and authoritative transmission of the school, primarily from the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (zahir al-riwaya), when can the buyer of a claimed item recourse against the guarantor of liability (damān al-darak)?
A) Immediately upon the claim (istihqāq) of the item by a third party.
B) Only after the court decrees that the seller must return the price to the buyer.
C) Only after the buyer has filed a lawsuit against the seller.
D) The buyer can never recourse against the guarantor; only against the seller.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor of liability (damān al-darak) is not liable until a court decree establishes the seller's obligation to return the price.

Joint Kitabah and Mutual Kafalah: Validity by Istihsan
Ruling on joint kitabah with mutual kafalah and the dispute with Zufar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on joint kitabah with mutual kafalah and evaluate the dispute with Zufar regarding when the slaves become free.

Core Idea
Joint kitabah with mutual kafalah is valid by istihsan; neither slave is freed until all money is paid.

In this passage, the author discusses a contract where a master writes a single kitabah for two slaves who guarantee each other. The kafalah appears invalid because the mukatab cannot give gratuitous guarantees and the kitabah consideration is not a valid debt. However, the Hanafi school validates it through istihsan by treating each slave as a principal for the entire amount, ensuring the master's condition is fulfilled. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with Zufar's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage to identify three distinct cases of joint kitabah: with mutual kafalah, without any kafalah, and with a condition of joint payment but no kafalah. Note which scenario is the main focus.
2
Understand the apparent invalidity of kafalah
Analyze why the kafalah seems invalid: (a) the mukatab is not eligible for gratuitous acts like kafalah, and (b) the badal al-kitabah is not a valid debt that can be guaranteed.
3
Grasp the istihsan validation
Study how the Hanafi school validates the contract by making each slave a principal for the entire amount in relation to the master, and a guarantor for his companion internally. This preserves the master's right to demand full payment from either.
The key principle: 'a person's transaction must be validated as much as possible'.
4
Analyze the dispute with Zufar
Contrast the Hanafi position with Zufar's view that each slave is freed by paying his own share. Understand Zufar's proof and the Hanafi rebuttal based on the master's condition and the istihsan construction.
Zufar is an early Hanafi scholar; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Recognize the practical consequence: under Hanafi view, the master secures the entire amount before any freedom; under Zufar's view, each slave can become free separately, risking the master's recovery.
