Rights of Opening Doors in Branching Alleyways
Ruling on house owners opening doors in a long alley with a branching non-through alley.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on opening doors in branching alleyways by analyzing the owner's right of passage and the alley's classification.

Core Idea
Opening a door in a non-through alley requires an existing right of passage; through alleys grant two-way access.

In classical Hanafi property law, the right to open a door into a shared alley is not absolute—it depends on whether the owner already has a right of passage (haqq al-murur) in that alley. This lesson examines a commentary from 'Amdat al-Ri'ayah that clarifies the ruling for houses situated at corners of a long alley (za'ighah mustatilah) branching into a shorter alley. By analyzing multiple scenarios, you will learn how the through/non-through nature of the alley determines the owner's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the typology of alleys
Distinguish between the long alley (tawilah), the side alley when it is non-through (ghayr nafidhah), and when it is through (nafidhah).
2
Map the houses and corners
Visualize the two corners: first corner near the start of the side alley, second corner deeper in. Identify houses 3,4,5,6.
3
Apply the right of passage rule
If a house's door faces the long alley, the owner has right of passage there; if facing the side alley, right only in that alley (unless it's through).
4
Derive the prohibition
If opening a new door in an alley where the owner has no right, it is prohibited because it would burden the neighbors.
5
Distinguish through vs non-through
In a through alley, the owner of any corner house has right of passage in both alleys, so can open doors in both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : زائغة مستطيلة الزائغةُ : الطريق الذي حاد عن الطريق الأعظم، من زاغت الشمس إذا مالت والمستطيلة : بمعنى الطويلة من استطال بمعنى طال. كذا في «البحر»(۲)، وصورتها هكذا : فالدَّارُ الثالثة التي في ركن المنشعبة الغير النافذة : لو كان بأبها في الطويلة يُمْنَعُ صاحبها من فتح الباب في المنشعبة الغير النافذة ؛ لأنه ليس له حق المرور فيها.
ولو كان بأبها في المنشعبة لا يمنع من فتح باب في الأولى الطويلة.
وأما الدار الرابعة التي في ركن الثاني :
لو كان بابها في الطويلة يمنعُ من فتحه في المنشعبة المذكورة.
وكذا لو كان في المنشعبة يمنع من فتحه في الطويلة ؛ لأنه ليس له حق المرور في ذلك الجانب، لكن هذا إذا كانت الطويلة غير نافذة ، بخلاف النافذة ؛ لأنَّ له حق المرور
حينئذ من الجانبين. وأما الدار الخامسة في الركن الأول من المنشعبة الثانية النافذة، فلصاحبها فتح الباب فيها، وفي الطويلة بخلاف الدار السادسة التي في الركن الثاني من المنشعبة المذكورة، فإنّه لو كان بابه فيها يمنع من الفتح في الطويلة لو كانت غير نافذة، ولا يمنع لو كانت نافذة.
His saying: 'a long alley branching from a main alley' – al-za'ighah is a road that deviates from the great road, from 'zaghat al-shams' when it inclines. Al-mustatilah means long. Thus the third house which is at the corner of the non-through branch: if its door is in the long [main] alley, its owner is prevented from opening a door in the non-through branch because he has no right of passage there. And if its door is in the branch, he is not prevented from opening a door in the first long alley. As for the fourth house which is at the second corner: if its door is in the long alley, he is prevented from opening it in the mentioned branch. Similarly, if it is in the branch, he is prevented from opening it in the long alley because he has no right of passage on that side. But this is when the long alley is not through; unlike if it is through, because then he has right of passage from both sides. As for the fifth house at the first corner of the second through branch, its owner may open a door in it and in the long alley. Unlike the sixth house which is at the second corner of the mentioned branch: if his door is in it, he is prevented from opening in the long alley if it were non-through, but not prevented if it were through.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage from 'Amdat al-Ri'ayah explains a ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding the rights of owners of houses located at corners of a long alley (za'ighah mustatilah) that branches into a shorter alley (munsha'ibah). The passage type is a ruling statement with a detailed description of multiple scenarios. The principle is that the right to open a door in a particular alley depends on whether the owner has a right of passage (haqq al-murur) in that alley. The commentator first defines 'za'ighah' as a road that deviates from the main road, and 'mustatilah' as long. He then describes the location of houses and their doors using a diagrammatic explanation (the actual diagram is not reproduced but the description is clear). There are three types of alleys: the long main alley (tawilah), the side alley that is non-through (ghayr nafidhah), and the side alley that is through (nafidhah). The houses are at corners. The ruling: If a house is at the corner of a non-through side alley and its door faces the long alley, the owner cannot open a new door in the side alley because he has no right of passage there. If his door already faces the side alley, he can open a door in the long alley. For a house at the opposite corner, the reverse applies. However, if the side alley is through (i.e., open at both ends), the owner has right of passage from both sides, so he can open doors in either alley. The commentator gives examples for six houses to illustrate the scenario. This ruling is based on the principle that one may only open a door into a shared alley if one has a legal right to use that alley; opening a new door may increase the burden on neighbors.

Key Terms
زائغة (za'ighah): A road that deviates from the main road; a side alley.
مستطيلة (mustatilah): Long, elongated; referring to the main alley.
منشعبة (munsha'ibah): A branch alley that splits from the main alley.
نافذة (nafidhah): An alley that is open at both ends, allowing passage through.
غير نافذة (ghayr nafidhah): An alley that is closed at one end, a dead end.
حق المرور (haqq al-murur): The legal right to pass through a particular alley.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house is at the corner of a non-through side alley (ghayr nafidhah) and its only door currently opens to the long main alley, can the owner open a second door into the side alley?
A) Yes, because he owns the house.
B) No, because he has no right of passage in the side alley.
C) Yes, if the side alley is wide enough.
D) No, because the side alley is private property.

Key Takeaway
The right to open a door into an alley depends on having a right of passage (haqq al-murur) in that alley, not merely on owning the house.

Rulings on Opening Doors in Non-Through Alleys
Explains when owners can open doors in various alley configurations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rulings to determine whether opening a new door in a shared alley is permitted or prohibited, based on alley type and the owner's right of passage.

Core Idea
Opening a door in a shared alley is prohibited unless you have a right of passage—the alley's shape and public access determine that right.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah delves into the fiqh of door-opening in non-through alleys. The Hanafi school presumes prohibition (asl al-hazr) due to the rights of alley residents, but makes nuanced exceptions based on alley geometry and the door's intended purpose. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for property owners and judges handling disputes in shared spaces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alley type
Determine whether the alley is a zā'ighah (branch alley). Classify its shape: long (mustatīlah), circular (mudawwarah), or square (murabba'ah). Also, is it through (nāfidhah) or non-through (ghayr nāfidhah)? This classification determines the default ruling.
A through alley leads to a public road; a non-through alley ends within the property.
2
Locate the house in relation to the alleys
Note the position of the house (e.g., corner of a branch) and which alley its existing door faces. The owner's right of passage is limited to the alleys he directly accesses.
For example, a house with its door in the long alley may have no right to open a door into the branch.
3
Apply the default ruling
For non-through alleys, the default is prohibition (yumna') unless the owner has a right of passage. For through alleys, passage is a public right, so restrictions are minimal.
The principle is asl al-hazr: any new door that affects shared rights is prohibited until proven harmless.
4
Consider the door's purpose
If the door is intended for passage, it is prohibited by istihsan. But if it is only for light and air (istiḍā'ah, rīḥ), it is not prohibited.
This distinction shows the harm depends on actual use, not the door itself.
5
Check for exceptions
Circular alleys (zā'ighah mudawwarah) are common property, so any owner may open a door anywhere. Square alleys (murabba'ah) are like an alley within an alley; outer alley residents cannot open into the square. Also, the opposing opinion (qīla) that permits based on the right to demolish the wall is rejected as-sahīh.
The correct position is the first: prohibition for passage without right.

If his door is in it, he is prevented from opening in the long alley if it is non-through, and he is not prevented if it is through.

Opening a door
As for the houses in the circular oblique alley, it is permissible to open their doors in that alley, in any place the owner wishes, because it is one alley and it is common property among them. Do you not see that they have the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) collectively? Unlike the houses in the square oblique alley, for the square oblique alley is like an alley within an alley. So the people of the first (alley) may not open (doors) into the square one. So remember.

[1] His saying: Opening a door – because the door is intended for passage, and they have no right to enter it because it is non-through. That right is exclusively for its residents. Do you not see that if a house from it is sold, the right of pre-emption belongs to them, not to the people of the first (alley)? Unlike if the branching oblique alley is through, because passage is a right of the public, and they are among them.
And it is said: They are not prevented from opening the door because he can demolish his entire wall, so it is more appropriate that he can demolish part. But the correct is the first. However, this is if he intends to open the door for passage. Then he is prevented by istihsan. And if he intends it for light and air without passage, he is not prevented.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, deals with the fiqh of opening doors in shared alleyways that are not through-streets (zā'ighah ghayr nāfidhah). The author begins by stating a general principle: the default ruling is prohibition (hazr) because opening a new door affects the rights of others in the alley. This is based on the fact that the alley is a shared space, and any change requires consent. When there is doubt about harm, the prohibition remains.

The passage then defines the key terms: 'zā'ighah mustatīlah' (a long oblique alley that branches off from a main road) and provides detailed scenarios for houses located at different points in such alleys. For example, if a house is at the corner of a non-through branch, the owner is prevented from opening a door into that branch if his main door is in the long alley, because he has no right of passage through the branch. Conversely, if his door is in the branch, he may open a door into the long alley.

The text distinguishes between through (nāfidhah) and non-through (ghayr nāfidhah) alleys. In a through alley, passage is a public right, so restrictions are fewer. For circular oblique alleys (zā'ighah mudawwarah), owners may open doors anywhere because the entire alley is common property. In square oblique alleys (zā'ighah murabba'ah), which are like an alley within an alley, the people of the outer alley cannot open doors into the inner square.

A key point: the ruling depends on the intention. If the door is for passage, it is prevented by istihsan (juristic preference). But if it is for light and air only, it is not prevented. The passage also mentions an alternative opinion (qīla) that permits opening doors based on the right to demolish walls, but the correct position (as-sahīh) is the first one that restricts opening for passage.

Key Terms
زائغة (zā'ighah): A road or alley that deviates from the main road; a branching offshoot.
مستطيلة (mustatīlah): Long, elongated; refers to a long alley.
نافذة (nāfidhah): A through road or alley that leads to a public thoroughfare; public right of passage exists.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption; the right of co-owners to buy a sold share before outsiders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house is in a non-through branch alley and its door is in the long alley, can the owner open a new door in the branch alley?
A) Yes, because he owns the house.
B) No, because he has no right of passage in the branch.
C) Yes, if it is for light and air.
D) Only if the branch is through.

Key Takeaway
Opening a new door for passage in a shared alley is prohibited unless the owner has a right of passage; the alley's shape and public access determine that right, with circular alleys being an exception as common property.

Permissibility of Intercourse When Buyer Denies Sale
Ruling on permissibility of intercourse for seller when buyer denies purchase and claimant drops suit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility of intercourse for a seller when the buyer denies the purchase and the suit is dropped, and explain the legal reasoning that the buyer's denial constitutes faskh.

Core Idea
When a buyer denies purchasing a slave-girl and the seller drops the suit, the sale is rescinded and the seller may have intercourse with her.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi sales law: a seller claims a buyer bought his slave-girl, but the buyer denies, and the seller abandons the lawsuit. The text rules that the seller may then have intercourse with the slave-girl. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this, focusing on the concept of rescission (faskh) implied by the buyer's denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
The seller claims the buyer purchased his slave-girl. The buyer denies the purchase. The seller then abandons the lawsuit.
The seller is the claimant, the buyer is the defendant.
2
Determine the ruling
The text states that it becomes permissible (halal) for the seller to have intercourse with the slave-girl.
This is a specific ruling, not a general rule.
3
Identify the legal reasoning
The commentary explains: since the buyer's denial makes it impossible for the seller to obtain the price, the seller's consent (rida) to the sale is gone. The buyer's denial itself is considered a rescission (faskh) from his side.
Thus the contract is effectively rescinded without formal court action.
4
Understand the concept of faskh by denial
In Hanafi law, a party's denial of a contract can operate as a rescission, especially when combined with the other party's acceptance of that denial (by dropping the suit).
This is a specific application of the principle that actions indicate consent.
5
Apply to analogous scenarios
Consider other contracts: if a seller claims a buyer purchased an item and the buyer denies, and the seller drops the claim, the seller may treat the item as his own.
The same reasoning applies to any sale, not just slave-girls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَن ادعى أن زيداً اشترى جاريته ، فأنكر ، وترك المدعي خصومتَهُ حلَّ له وطؤها (ومن ادعى أن زيداً اشترى جاريته ، فأنكر ، وترك المدعي خصومتهُ حلَّ له وطؤها ; لأنَّه إذا تعدَّرَ للبائع حصولُ الثَّمن من المشتري فات رضاء البائع فيستبد بفسخه لا سيما إذا جَحَدَ المشتري ، فإنَّ جحوده فسخ من جهته.)
And whoever claims that Zayd bought his slave-girl, and he denies, and the claimant abandons his lawsuit, it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with her. (And whoever claims that Zayd bought his slave-girl, and he denies, and the claimant abandons his lawsuit, it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with her, because when the seller's obtaining of the price from the buyer becomes impossible, the seller's consent is gone, so he independently rescinds, especially if the buyer denies, for his denial is a rescission from his side.)
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: If a person claims that Zayd bought his slave-girl, and Zayd denies, and the claimant (the seller) then drops the lawsuit, the seller is permitted to have intercourse with his slave-girl. The commentary explains the reasoning: Since the buyer denies the purchase, the seller cannot obtain the price, so the seller's consent (rida) to the sale is gone. The seller may then rescind the contract unilaterally (istibdal bi faskh). The buyer's denial itself is considered a rescission (faskh) from his side. This is a Hanafi ruling in sales and judicial procedure.

Key Terms
جرة (jariyah): A female slave.
خصومة (khusumah): Lawsuit or legal dispute.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, annulment.

B) He may have intercourse because the sale is rescinded.
C) He must get a judicial decree of rescission first.
D) He may sell the slave-girl to someone else.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's denial of a sale, combined with the seller dropping the suit, amounts to a rescission (faskh), restoring the seller's full ownership rights, including the right to intercourse.

Buyer's Denial as Rescission in Sale
Ruling on seller's recourse when buyer denies purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the buyer denies a sale and the seller drops the claim, and explain the legal reasoning (illa) that permits unilateral rescission.

Core Idea
A buyer's denial of purchase is itself a rescission, making the seller's unilateral rescission valid.

Imagine you sell something, but the buyer later denies ever buying it. What are your rights? This lesson explores a specific Hanafi ruling: if the buyer denies the sale and the seller abandons his claim, the seller may retake the item and even use it. The reasoning is grounded in the impossibility of collecting the price and the buyer's own denial constituting a rescission. Let's examine the legal logic behind this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The text presents a case: someone claims Zayd bought his slave-girl, Zayd denies, and the seller drops the lawsuit. Identify the parties and the claim.
Note that 'slave-girl' is a chattel; today this applies to any sold item.
2
State the Ruling
What is the legal consequence? The seller may lawfully have intercourse with the slave-girl, meaning he retakes full ownership and the sale is rescinded.
This is a statement of the Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the Reasoning (Illa)
Why is rescission allowed? Because obtaining the price becomes impossible (the buyer denies), so the seller's consent to the sale is lost. Additionally, the buyer's denial itself is considered a rescission from his side.
The reasoning is rational (dalil aqli) based on the purpose of the contract.
4
Note the Practical Effect
The seller does not need a court order to rescind; it is a unilateral right. This differentiates Hanafi from some other schools.
This is a key practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ادعى أن زيداً اشترى جاريته ، فأنكر ، وترك المدعي خصومتهُ حلَّ له وطؤها ؛ لأنَّه إذا تعذّرَ للبائع حصولُ الثَّمن من المشتري فات رضاء البائع فيستبد بفسخه لا سيما إذا جَحَدَ المشتري ، فإنَّ جحوده فسخ من جهته.
And whoever claims that Zayd bought his slave-girl, and he (Zayd) denied it, and the claimant dropped his lawsuit, then it becomes permissible for him (the seller) to have intercourse with her; because when obtaining the price from the buyer becomes impossible, the seller's consent ceases, so he may unilaterally rescind, especially when the buyer denies, for his denial is a rescission on his part.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: if a seller claims that Zayd bought his slave-girl, but Zayd denies the purchase, and the seller abandons his claim, then the seller may lawfully have intercourse with the slave-girl (i.e., he retakes full ownership). The reasoning is that when the buyer denies the contract, it becomes impossible for the seller to obtain the price. Since the seller's consent to the sale was based on receiving the price, that consent is lost. Therefore, the seller may unilaterally rescind the contract. This is especially clear when the buyer denies, as his denial itself is considered a rescission from his side.

Key Terms
جحد (jahada): To deny; in this context, the buyer denying the contract.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a buyer denies purchasing a slave-girl and the seller drops the claim?
A) The seller loses ownership
B) The seller may rescind and retake the slave-girl
C) The slave-girl goes free
D) The seller must pay compensation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a buyer's denial of purchase, combined with the seller dropping the claim, gives the seller the right to rescind the contract unilaterally.

Classification of Dirhams: Jiyad, Zayf, Nabahrajah, and Sutooqah
Defining the four categories of dirhams and their legal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and distinguish the four categories of dirhams (jiyad, zayf, nabahrajah, sutooqah) and state the basic ruling on their permissibility in transactions.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school classifies dirhams into four types: jiyad (pure), zayf (debased but acceptable), nabahrajah (rejected by merchants), and sutooqah (counterfeit, not a dirham).

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the legal classification of dirhams in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding these categories is essential for transactions, zakah, and especially for contracts like sarf and salam. The text not only defines each type but also lays out when claims about coin quality are legally accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Find the four categories of dirhams mentioned in the text: jiyad, zayf, nabahrajah, and sutooqah. Note their distinct characteristics.
2
Understand the characteristics
For each type, determine the silver content, acceptance by treasury and merchants, and whether it is considered a dirham.
Jiyad is pure silver; zayf has less silver but is still dominant; nabahrajah is inferior; sutooqah is base metal.
3
Note legal consequences
Identify which types can be used in transactions such as sarf and salam. Jiyad and zayf are permissible; nabahrajah is not accepted by merchants; sutooqah invalidates contracts.
The treasury only accepts jiyad.
4
Recognize sutooqah as non-dirham
Understand that sutooqah is not of the genus of dirhams, so claims of sutooqah are not believed and its use in sarf/salam is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا من ادعى أنها سَتُّوقةٌ، ولا مَن أقرَّ بقبض الجياد، أو حقه، أو الثمن أو الاستيفاء، والزيفُ رد لبيت المال كالنَّبَهْرَجَةِ للتجار، والسوقةُ ما غَلَبَ عليه غَشُهُ لا من ادعى أنها ستوقةٌ، ولا مَن أقرَّ بقبض الجياد، أو حقه، أو الثَّمن أو الاستيفاء ) : أي إذا قال استوفيتُ منه عشرة دراهم ؛ لأن الاستيفاء يدل على الكمال.
والزيف ردّ لبيت المال كالنَّبَهْرَجَةِ للتجار، والسوقةُ ما غَلَبَ عليه غشه) : الزَّيْفُ والنَّبَهْرَجَةُ من جنس الدَّراهم التي الفضةُ غالبة على الغش إِلا أَنَّهَا بالنسبة إلى الجيد يكون فضتُهما أقل، إلا أنَّ رداءة الزَّيفِ دون رداءةِ النَّبَهْرَجَة ، فالزيف لا يَرُدُّه التجار، ويجري فيه المعاملة إلا أن بيت المال لا يقبلُه ، فإنَّ بيت المال لا يقبل إلا ما هو جيد غاية الجودة.
والنبهرجة يردُّهُ التَّجَارُ ، والنَّبَهْرَجُ الباطلُ والرَّدِئ من الشيء، والدرهم النبهرج : قيل : ما بطل سكته ، وقيل : الذي فضتُه رديئة ، وقيل : الغالب الفضة، وهو معرب نبهره
تجوز بهما فيما لا يجوز الاستبدال به كالصرف والسَّلَم جاز، ولو لم تكن من جنس الدراهم كان التجوز استبدالاً ، وهو فيهما لا يجوز ، ولو ادعى أنها ستوقة لم يصدق ؛ لأن الستوقة ليست من جنس الدراهم ؛ ولهذا لو تجوز بهما في الصرف والسلم لم يجز. كذا في أكثر الكتب. وقال في «المنح : لو ادّعى أنها سَتُّوقة لا يصدق إن كان البيان مفصولاً، وصدق لو كان البيان منه موصولاً ، كما في «النهاية» وغيره، وإن أقر أنه قبض حقه أو الثمن أو استوفى حقه صُدّق في دعواه الزيافة لو كان بيانه ذلك . موصولاً، وإلا لا. والفرق أنَّ في المسائل الثلاث أقرّ بقبض القدر والجودة بلفظ واحد، فإذا استثنى الجودة كان استثناء البعض من الكل، فصح موصولاً بقوله : له علي ألف إلا مئة. أما إذا أقرّ بقبض عشرة جياد، فقد أتى بكل منهما بلفظ على حدة، فإذا قال : لا إنها زيوف، فقد استثنى الكلَّ من الكلِّ في حق الجودة، وهو باطل، كقوله : له عليَّ مئة درهم ودينار إلا دينارا ، كان باطلاً ، إن كان موصولا. كذا في «النهاية» انتهى.
Neither the one who claims they are sutooqah, nor the one who acknowledges receipt of jiyad, or his right, or the price, or istifa (full receipt) - and the zayf is returned to the treasury like nabahrajah to merchants, and as-suuqa is that whose adulteration is predominant - nor the one who claims they are sutooqah, nor the one who acknowledges receipt of jiyad, or his right, or the price, or istifa): meaning when he says 'I have received ten dirhams from him' because istifa indicates completeness. And zayf is returned to the treasury like nabahrajah to merchants, and as-suuqa is that whose adulteration is predominant. Zayf and nabahrajah are a type of dirham in which silver is predominant over adulteration, but compared to jiyad their silver is less, except that the inferiority of zayf is less than that of nabahrajah. Thus, zayf is not rejected by merchants, and transactions take place with it, except that the treasury does not accept it; indeed the treasury does not accept anything except what is of the highest quality. And nabahrajah is rejected by merchants. An-nabahraj is the false and worthless of a thing, and the nabahraj dirham: it is said that whose stamp is voided, and it is said that whose silver is inferior, and it is said that the predominant is silver, and it is the arabicization of 'nabahrah'. It is permissible to use them as a substitute where substitution is not allowed like in sarf (currency exchange) and salam, but if they were not of the type of dirhams, the utilization would be a substitution, and in them it is not permissible. And if he claimed they were sutooqah, he is not believed because sutooqah are not of the type of dirhams; therefore, if he utilized them in sarf and salam it is not permissible. Thus in most books. And it is said in al-Minah: If he claimed they were sutooqah, he is not believed if the statement was separated (munfasil), and he is believed if the statement was connected (muttasil), as in an-Nihayah and others. And if he acknowledged that he received his right or the price or was paid in full, he is believed in his claim of zayf if his statement was connected; otherwise not. And the difference is that in the three issues he acknowledged receipt of the quantity and quality with one word; when he excludes the quality, it is an exception of a part from the whole, so it is valid when connected, as his statement: 'He has a thousand except one hundred against me.' As for when he acknowledged receipt of ten jiyad, he has come with each with a separate word; thus when he says 'No, they are zayuf,' he has excluded the whole from the whole in terms of quality, which is invalid, like his statement: 'He has one hundred dirhams and a dinar except a dinar against me,' it is invalid if it was connected. Thus in an-Nihayah, end.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the types of dirhams and the legal rulings concerning claims of defect in coins. It first distinguishes four categories: jiyad (good, pure silver coins accepted by both treasury and merchants), zayf (debased but with silver predominant; rejected by treasury but used by merchants), nabahrajah (worse than zayf, rejected even by merchants), and sutooqah (base metal with silver coating, not considered dirhams at all). The passage then addresses two legal scenarios. First, if someone claims that coins he received are sutooqah, he is not believed because sutooqah are not dirhams; this would void the transaction. Second, if someone acknowledges receipt of jiyad (or his right, price, or complete payment) and later claims the coins were actually zayf, his claim is accepted only if his qualifying statement is made immediately (mutassil) and in a single utterance; if it is separate (munfasil), it is considered a contradiction. The reasoning is that in the latter case, acknowledging quantity and quality separately makes the later exception a self-contradiction, whereas in the former case, the exception in one utterance is like an exception from a whole. This is a detailed technical ruling in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الجياد (al-jiyad): Good quality dirhams of pure silver, accepted by both the treasury (bayt al-mal) and merchants.
الزيف (az-zayf): Dirhams with less silver than jiyad but still silver-dominant; accepted by merchants but rejected by the treasury.
النَّبَهْرَجَة (an-nabahrajah): Dirhams of inferior quality, silver not dominant; rejected by merchants and considered false currency.
السَّتُّوقَة (as-sutooqah): Base metal coated with silver, not considered a dirham at all; its use in sarf or salam invalidates the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of dirham is accepted by both the treasury and merchants?
A) Zayf
B) Jiyad
C) Nabahrajah
D) Sutooqah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the legal status of a coin transaction depends on the category of dirham used; sutooqah is not considered a dirham and invalidates sarf and salam.

Legal Effect of Claims on Coin Quality: Muttasil vs Munfasil
Rules for claims of coin defects: muttasil vs munfasil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on claims of coin defects: a claim of zayf after acknowledging jiyad is accepted only if the statement is immediate and connected (muttasil), not separate (munfasil), and only if the acknowledgment was generic.

Core Idea
A claim that received coins were defective is only legally effective if the qualifying statement is made in the same breath as a generic acknowledgment.

This passage delves into procedural fairness in claims about coin quality. If a person first acknowledges receipt of jiyad (good coins) and later claims they were actually zayf, the timing of the claim matters. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a connected statement (muttasil) - which is akin to an exception from a whole - and a separate statement (munfasil) - which is seen as a self-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Identify that the text deals with a situation where a person acknowledges receipt of payment (e.g., his right, the price, or full payment) and then claims the coins were zayf.
2
Distinguish between connected and separate statements
Note that if the claim is made immediately (muttasil) in the same utterance, it is treated as an exception. If made later (munfasil), it is seen as a contradiction.
The text uses the analogy of 'I have a thousand except one hundred.'
3
Apply the exception analogy
When the acknowledgment is generic (e.g., 'I received my right'), quantity and quality are implied together. Excluding the quality (zayf) is like excluding a part from the whole, valid if muttasil.
But if he specifically says 'ten jiyad', quantity and quality are separate, so excluding quality is like excluding the whole from the whole, invalid even if muttasil.
4
Conclude when the claim is believed
A claim of zayf is believed only if the acknowledgment was generic and the qualification is muttasil. If the acknowledgment specified jiyad, the claim is never accepted.

Source Text

Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the types of dirhams and the legal rulings concerning claims of defect in coins. It first distinguishes four categories: jiyad (good, pure silver coins accepted by both treasury and merchants), zayf (debased but with silver predominant; rejected by treasury but used by merchants), nabahrajah (worse than zayf, rejected even by merchants), and sutooqah (base metal with silver coating, not considered dirhams at all). The passage then addresses two legal scenarios. First, if someone claims that coins he received are sutooqah, he is not believed because sutooqah are not dirhams; this would void the transaction. Second, if someone acknowledges receipt of jiyad (or his right, price, or complete payment) and later claims the coins were actually zayf, his claim is accepted only if his qualifying statement is made immediately (mutassil) and in a single utterance; if it is separate (munfasil), it is considered a contradiction. The reasoning is that in the latter case, acknowledging quantity and quality separately makes the later exception a self-contradiction, whereas in the former case, the exception in one utterance is like an exception from a whole. This is a detailed technical ruling in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المتصل (al-muttasil): A qualifying statement made immediately and connected to the initial acknowledgment in the same utterance.
المنفصل (al-munfasil): A qualifying statement made separately, after a pause or in a different utterance.
الجياد (al-jiyad): Good quality dirhams of pure silver, accepted by both the treasury and merchants.
الزيف (az-zayf): Dirhams with less silver than jiyad but still silver-dominant; accepted by merchants but rejected by the treasury.
السَّتُّوقَة (as-sutooqah): Base metal coated with silver, not considered a dirham at all; its use in sarf or salam invalidates the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person acknowledges receiving ten jiyad (good dirhams) in one breath and then immediately says 'actually they are zayf,' is his claim accepted?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Only if the seller agrees
D) Only if the coins are still in hand

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a claim that received coins were defective is only legally valid if the qualification is made in the same breath as a generic acknowledgment; if the good quality is explicitly stated first, the claim is rejected.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
Types of Dirhams: Definitions and Legal Status
Definitions and rulings on coin purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each of the four types of dirhams (jayyid, zuyuf, nahrja, sutwa) and state their permissibility in trade according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Only pure silver dirhams (jayyid) are full legal currency; adulterated and plated coins have restricted or no legal standing.

In the Hanafi school, silver coins are not all equal. The scholars classified dirhams based on purity and acceptability by the treasury and merchants. Understanding these categories is essential for correct transactions, zakat calculation, and avoiding fraud. This lesson unpacks the four types as defined in the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms
List the four types mentioned: al-jiyad, al-zuyuf, al-nahrja, al-sutwa. Note the Arabic and English transliteration.
Each term has a specific meaning related to purity and acceptance.
2
Extract the definition for each
From the passage, write down the definition given for each type. Pay attention to the material composition and who accepts/rejects them.
Definitions are based on majority of mashayikh.
3
Determine the legal status
For each type, identify what the scholars say about using it in trade. Which are permissible? Which are not?
4
Note the conditions
If using zuyuf, what condition does the text mention? Why is sutwa considered to have no legal status?
5
Reflect on the rationale
Consider why the school distinguishes between pure and impure coins. What is the underlying principle?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في تفسير النبهرجة، قيل : هي التي تضرب في غير دار السلطان، والزيوف : هي المغشوشة، والسوقة : صفر مموّه بالفضة، وقال عامة المشايخ : الجياد : فضة خالصة تروج في التجارات، وتوضع في بيت المال. والزيوفُ ما زيفه : أي ردّه بيت المال، ولكن يأخذه التجار في التجارات، ولا بأس بالشراء بها ، لكن ينبغي أن يبين البائع أنها زيوف، والنَّهْرَجةُ ما يردُّه التجار، والسوقة: أن يكون الطاقُ الأعلى والأسفل ،فضة ، وبينهما صفر، وليس لهما حكم الدراهم. كذا في «الخانية» وغيرها ، وقد حققنا ذلك فيما سبق.
And they differed in interpretation of al-nahrja. It is said: it is that which is minted outside the sultan's mint. And al-zuyuf: they are the adulterated ones. And al-sutwa: copper plated with silver. And the majority of mashayikh said: al-jiyad: pure silver that circulates in trade and is placed in the treasury. And al-zuyuf: what is rejected, i.e., the treasury rejects it, but merchants accept it in trade, and there is no harm in buying with it, but the seller should state that they are zuyuf. And al-nahrja: what merchants reject. And al-sutwa: that the upper and lower layers are silver, and between them is copper, and they have no legal status of dirhams. Thus in al-Khaniyyah and others, and we have verified that previously.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage from the sharh discusses the classification of dirhams (silver coins) based on purity and acceptability. The four types are: (1) Jayyid (جيد): pure silver coins that are widely accepted in trade and can be placed in the public treasury (bayt al-mal). These are the standard legal currency. (2) Zuyuf (زيوف): counterfeit or adulterated coins that the treasury rejects but merchants may accept in transactions. It is permissible to buy and sell with them, but the seller should disclose their low quality to avoid deceit. (3) Nahrja (نبهرجة): coins that even merchants reject, possibly due to very low silver content or being minted unofficially. (4) Sutwa (سوقة): coins with silver layers on the outside but copper inside; such coins have no legal status as dirhams and presumably cannot be used as currency. The passage notes scholarly differences on the exact definitions but gives the view of the majority of mashayikh as the relied-upon one. This classification has practical implications for zakah calculation, sales, and other financial transactions where the type of currency matters.

Key Terms
الجياد (al-jiyād): Pure silver coins that circulate freely in trade and are acceptable in the treasury.
الزيوف (al-zuyūf): Adulterated coins that the treasury rejects but merchants may accept if disclosed.
النبهاجة (al-nahrjah): Coins that both the treasury and merchants reject, possibly due to very low silver content.
السوقة (al-sutwa): Coins with silver on the outside and copper inside; they have no legal status as dirhams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which type of dirham is rejected by both the treasury and merchants?
A) Al-jiyad
B) Al-zuyuf
C) Al-nahrja
D) Al-sutwa

Key Takeaway
Only jayyid dirhams are full legal currency; zuyuf are permissible with disclosure, while nahrja and sutwa have no currency status.
